The True Meaning of Thanksgiving (Shukr)
Sanusi defines the true meaning of shukr as disseminating the blessing and making it known, with supporting hadith and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the true meaning of shukr (thankfulness) as the dissemination of blessings and making them known, and why praise is the head of thankfulness.

Core Idea
True thankfulness means publicly spreading the blessing, not hiding it.

Sanusi now defines the real meaning of shukr, moving beyond inner feeling to outward action. He explains that thankfulness consists of spreading the blessing and making it known, and its opposite is concealing it. The hadith and commentary attached highlight why verbal praise is considered the head of thankfulness—because it reveals and publicizes the blessing. This passage clarifies that gratitude is not merely internal but requires active articulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of shukr
Sanusi defines shukr as 'إشاعة النعمة والإبانة عنها' (disseminating the blessing and making it known). Note that this is an active, outward process.
The opposite of shukr is concealment, though not explicitly named in the passage.
2
Study the supporting hadith and commentary
The hadith states that praise is the head of thankfulness. Al-Tibi's commentary explains why verbal praise is the head: it spreads the blessing and is clearer than hidden belief or ambiguous actions.
This reinforces that tongue-praise is not just a component but the leader of shukr.
3
Reflect on the practical implication
Ask yourself how you can apply this definition in daily life—by verbally acknowledging and sharing Allah's blessings with others.
This challenges the common misconception that gratitude is only felt in the heart.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شك أنَّ مولانا جل وعزَّ مُثْنى عليه بالقلوب ؛ بما عرفتَهُ مِنْ كمال ذاته وصفاتِهِ ، وأَيَّدَتْ ذلكَ العقولُ بالأدلة القاطعة انزاحَتْ عنها عقائدُ الفسادِ التي أنتجها مخص التوهمات والتخيلاتِ وهذا أعظم مراتب الثناء وأفضلُهُ . وهو جلَّ وعلا مُثْنى عليه أيضاً بالألسنةِ ؛ بما تُقِرُّ بِهِ مِن انفرادِهِ تعالى بصفاتِ الجلال والكمال وما اعترفتْ بهِ مِنَ العبودية لهُ والتسليم لأقداره ، والشهادةِ لهُ بالوحدانيَّةِ ، ولا شكَّ أَنَّهُ ما شكرَ اللهَ تعالى عبد لم يحمده بلسانه على ما جاءَ بِهِ الحديثُ (۱) ؛ لأنَّ المنبى عما في الضمير وضعاً والمظهر له حقاً . . هو النطقُ . وحقيقة معنى الشكر : إشاعةُ النعمة والإبانة عنها ، ونقيضُه - وهو (1) منه ما روى البيهقي في شعب الإيمان ( ٤٠٨٥ ) من حديث سيدنا عبد الله بن عمرو رضي الله عنهما مرفوعاً : ( الحمد رأس الشكرِ ، ما شكر الله عبد لا يحمده » ، قال العلامة الطيبي في ( شرح المشكاة » ( ١٨٢٦/٦ ) : ( وإنَّما جعل الحمد رأساً ؛ لأن ذكر النعمة باللسان والثناء على موليها أشيع لها ، وأدلُّ على مكانها من الاعتقاد وآداب الجوارح ؛ لخفاء عمل القلب ، وما في عمل الجوارح من الاحتمال ، بخلاف عمل اللسان ؛ وهو النطق الذي يفصح عن كل خفي، ويجلي عن كل مشتبه ) ، وهو ما سيبينه المصنف في حقيقة معنى الشكر
And there is no doubt that our Lord, Glorious and Exalted, is praised in hearts, by what you have known of the perfection of His essence and attributes, and the intellects have confirmed that with conclusive proofs that dispel the corrupt beliefs produced by pure delusions and illusions. And this is the highest rank of praise and its best. And He, Exalted and Supreme, is also praised with tongues, by what they acknowledge of His exclusivity in attributes of majesty and perfection, and what they confess of servitude to Him, submission to His decrees, and testimony to His oneness. And there is no doubt that a servant has not thanked God if he has not praised Him with his tongue, according to what the hadith has brought; because the tongue is that which, by primal institution, declares what is in the heart and is its true revealer. And the true meaning of thankfulness is the dissemination of the blessing and making it known, and its opposite is—and it is (1) Among it is what al-Bayhaqi narrated in 'Shu'ab al-Iman' (4085) from the hadith of our master Abdullah ibn Amr, may God be pleased with them both, raised to the Prophet: 'Praise is the head of thankfulness: no servant thanks God who does not praise Him.' Al-Tibi said in 'Sharh al-Mishkat' (6/1826): 'He made praise the head because mentioning the blessing with the tongue and praising its bestower makes it more widespread and more indicative of its status than belief and the etiquette of the limbs, due to the concealment of the heart's action and the possibility in the action of the limbs, unlike the action of the tongue, which is speech that reveals every hidden thing and clarifies every ambiguity.' And this is what the author will explain in the true meaning of thankfulness.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the praise of God with heart and tongue and defines the true meaning of shukr. The author states that God is praised in hearts through knowledge of His perfection, supported by rational proofs that refute corrupt beliefs. This inward praise is the highest form. He is also praised with tongues through verbal acknowledgment of His attributes, servitude, submission, and testimony of His oneness. The author then asserts, based on a hadith, that a person has not thanked God if they have not praised Him with their tongue, because the tongue is the primary means to express what is in the heart. Finally, he defines the true meaning of shukr as disseminating the blessing and making it known, implying its opposite is concealment. The footnote provides the hadith and a commentary by al-Tibi explaining why praise with the tongue is considered the head of thankfulness due to its clarity and power to spread the blessing.

Key Terms
إشاعة النعمة
الإبانة
الشكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that shukr is only felt in the heart or shown through actions like prayer and fasting.
Correction: The passage defines shukr as specifically 'disseminating the blessing and making it known,' i.e., verbal acknowledgment and spreading awareness.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the true meaning of shukr?
A) Spreading the blessing and making it known
B) Praising God in silence
C) Giving charity
D) Fasting

Key Takeaway
True thankfulness means actively publicizing Allah's blessings through verbal praise and acknowledgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a specific blessing from Allah that you have received today. Have you voiced it aloud to anyone, even to yourself? If not, what is stopping you from making it known? Before you finish this reflection, say 'Alhamdulillah' for that blessing and commit to telling someone about it before the day ends.
?
The opposite of shukr is concealing blessings. Is there a blessing you have hidden because you feared envy or pride? Examine your heart: are you protecting your ego or failing to fulfill gratitude? Write down one blessing you will openly attribute to Allah, even if it feels vulnerable.
?
If praise is the head of thankfulness, then every time you withhold verbal praise, you are beheading your gratitude. When you are tested with hardship, do you still find words to praise Allah? What would it mean for your relationship with Him if you made a habit of saying 'Alhamdulillah' for every circumstance?

Praise by Heart, Tongue, and Limbs
Sanusi discusses praise by heart, tongue, and limbs, with a warning against hypocrisy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different modes of praise of Allah by creation: the heart, tongue, and limbs, and the importance of sincerity in verbal praise.

Core Idea
True praise begins with the heart, extends to the tongue, and is embodied by the limbs.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the relationship between inner gratitude and outward expression. He warns that verbal praise without heartfelt sincerity is mere hypocrisy, and then shows how every limb of the body can become a means of praise through obedience. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding the comprehensive nature of thanā'.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of kufran and shukr
Read how Sanusi defines ingratitude as covering, and why restricting gratitude to the heart involves a kind of concealment.
This sets up the need for verbal expression.
2
Examine the relationship between heart and tongue
Notice that verbal praise is a branch of inner gratitude. It derives honor from its source, but becomes hypocrisy if disconnected.
3
Discover praise by the limbs
See how all limbs can offer praise through submission, obedience, and devotion to Allah, preferring Him over all else.
This expands praise beyond mere words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفران - يُنبئ عن السَّترِ والتغطية ، ولا شكٍّ أنَّ الاقتصارَ في الشكرِ على القلبِ فيه نوعٌ مِنَ التغطية ، إلا أنَّ هذا الثناء باللسانِ وإِنْ كانَ لهُ مِنَ الشرفِ ما عُلِمَ .. فهو فرعٌ عنِ الأَوَّلِ ، وبنشأتهِ عنه يستحقُ الشرف ، وإلا كان نفاقاً . وهو تعالى مُثْنى عليه أيضاً بسائر الأركانِ ؛ بما انقادَتْ بهِ مِنَ الاستسلام والطاعة لأوامرِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ ، والعكوف على خدمته تعالى ، وإيثاره جل وعزَّ عن كل ما سواه . ومُثْنى عليهِ أيضاً بالأحوالِ ؛ بما أثبتته شواهد الفطرة ، وعلامة الحدوث في جميع العوالم جملة وتفصيلاً مِنْ عظيم افتقارها على كلِّ حال إليه جل وعزَّ ، وبما أثبتَتْهُ مِنْ وجوبِ وجودِهِ تعالى وكمال علمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياتِهِ ، وغير وغير ذلكَ ممَّا هو مُقرَّر في محله ، وهذا النوع من الثناء في العالم أعم ممَّا هو قبله . وإليه الإشارة بقولِهِ سبحانه وتعالى : ( وَإِن مِّن شَيْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ) [الإسراء : ٤٤] . فتبيَّنَ مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ﴾ - ولم يقلْ ولكن لا تسمعونَ - أَنَّهُ تسبيحُ يُفْقَهُ بالقلوب الزكيَّةِ ذواتِ الأنظارِ السديدة ، ولا يُسمَعُ بالآذانِ . فقدِ استبانَ بما ذُكِرَ : أَنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو المحمودُ المُثْنى عليه بلسانِ الحال ، وبلسان المقال ، وبالجَنَانِ والأركانِ ، مع التعذُّرِ أنْ يفي ذلكَ كلُّهُ بالنَّزْرِ اليسير ممَّا لا يتناهى مِنْ صفاتِ الجلال والجمال . اللهمَّ؛ لا أحصي ثناءً عليكَ ، أنتَ كما أثنيتَ على نفسِكَ . هذا مما يتعلَّق بحمدِ الخَلْقِ وثنائهم عليهِ تعالى .
Kufran (ingratitude) indicates covering and concealment. There is no doubt that restricting gratitude to the heart contains a kind of concealment, except that this praise with the tongue, even if it has the honor that is known... it is a branch of the first, and by its originating from it it deserves honor, otherwise it would be hypocrisy. And He, exalted, is also praised with all the limbs; by what they yield of submission and obedience to His commands, glory be to Him, and devotion to His service, and preferring Him over all else. And He is also praised by the [various] states; by what the evidences of innate disposition (fitra) have established, and the sign of temporality in all worlds collectively and in detail, from their immense need in every state for Him, glorified and exalted, and by what they have established of the necessity of His existence, exalted, and the perfection of His knowledge, power, will, and life, and other than that which is established in its place. And this type of praise in the world is more general than what came before it. And this is indicated by His saying, exalted: 'And there is no thing but that it glorifies with His praise, but you do not understand their glorification' [al-Isra:44]. So it became clear from His saying, exalted: 'but you do not understand their glorification' – and He did not say 'but you do not hear' – that it is a glorification understood by pure hearts with sound insights, and not heard by ears. So it became evident from what was mentioned that He, glorified and exalted, is the praised one, praised by the tongue of [outward] state, and by the tongue of speech, and by the hearts and limbs, while it is impossible for all of that to do justice to even the least portion of the infinite attributes of majesty and beauty. O Allah, I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself. This is what relates to the praise of creation and their praise of Him.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the various ways in which creation offers praise (thanā') to Allah. It begins by distinguishing between ingratitude (kufrān) as covering and gratitude (shukr) of the heart, which still involves concealment, and then explains that verbal praise is more explicit but must proceed from sincerity to avoid hypocrisy. The author then expands the discussion to include praise by the limbs through obedience and submission, and praise by the very states (aḥwāl) of all contingent beings. These states testify to their absolute need for Allah and to His necessary existence and perfect attributes. The author notes that this latter type of praise is more universal, as it encompasses all things. The verse from Surah al-Isrā' (17:44) is quoted to show that all things glorify Allah, but their glorification is understood by pure hearts with sound insights, not necessarily heard by ears. The passage concludes that despite these multiple modes of praise—by state, speech, heart, and limbs—they are utterly insufficient to account for even a fraction of Allah's infinite attributes of majesty and beauty, prompting the supplication: 'O Allah, I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.'

Key Terms
كفران
شكر
ثناء
لسان المقال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why can restricting gratitude to the heart alone be considered a form of kufran?
A) Because it denies Allah's blessings.
B) Because it involves covering up gratitude inwardly.
C) Because the heart is incapable of gratitude.
D) Because it leads to hypocrisy.

Key Takeaway
Praise of Allah is not only verbal but stems from the heart and manifests through the limbs in obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah with your tongue, do you ever stop to check if your heart is truly feeling that praise? If not, what does that say about the sincerity of your worship?
?
Your limbs perform acts of obedience—prayer, charity, fasting. But are they truly praising Allah through submission, or are they going through motions? What is one action you can change today to make your limbs more fully engaged in praise?
?
Sanusi warns that verbal praise without heart is hypocrisy. Where in your daily life does your tongue praise but your heart is distant? What will you do to align them starting with your next prayer?

Praise by States and the Universal Glorification
Sanusi explains praise by the states of creation and the cosmic glorification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how all created things praise Allah through their very states of contingency and need, and how this glorification is understood by pure hearts.

Core Idea
The entire universe glorifies Allah by its existence and dependence on Him.

Sanusi now takes us deeper: beyond human speech and actions, every created thing—by its very nature of coming into existence and being perpetually in need—praises Allah. This type of praise is universal and revealed in a Qur'anic verse. The lesson explains how this glorification is perceived not with ears but with the heart's insight.

Guided Path
1
Identify praise by states (ahwal)
Read how the signs of fitra and the mark of temporality (hadath) establish that all worlds are utterly dependent on Allah in every state.
This is the evidence for praise.
2
Understand the universality of this praise
Note that this type of praise is more general than the previous kinds—it encompasses everything.
3
Reflect on the Qur'anic proof
Examine verse 17:44 and Sanusi's insight that Allah did not say 'you do not hear' but 'you do not understand', indicating a spiritual perception.
4
Conclude with human limitation
Appreciate the supplication that no praise can fully encompass Allah's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكفران - يُنبئ عن السَّترِ والتغطية ، ولا شكٍّ أنَّ الاقتصارَ في الشكرِ على القلبِ فيه نوعٌ مِنَ التغطية ، إلا أنَّ هذا الثناء باللسانِ وإِنْ كانَ لهُ مِنَ الشرفِ ما عُلِمَ .. فهو فرعٌ عنِ الأَوَّلِ ، وبنشأتهِ عنه يستحقُ الشرف ، وإلا كان نفاقاً . وهو تعالى مُثْنى عليه أيضاً بسائر الأركانِ ؛ بما انقادَتْ بهِ مِنَ الاستسلام والطاعة لأوامرِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ ، والعكوف على خدمته تعالى ، وإيثاره جل وعزَّ عن كل ما سواه . ومُثْنى عليهِ أيضاً بالأحوالِ ؛ بما أثبتته شواهد الفطرة ، وعلامة الحدوث في جميع العوالم جملة وتفصيلاً مِنْ عظيم افتقارها على كلِّ حال إليه جل وعزَّ ، وبما أثبتَتْهُ مِنْ وجوبِ وجودِهِ تعالى وكمال علمه وقدرته وإرادته وحياتِهِ ، وغير وغير ذلكَ ممَّا هو مُقرَّر في محله ، وهذا النوع من الثناء في العالم أعم ممَّا هو قبله . وإليه الإشارة بقولِهِ سبحانه وتعالى : ( وَإِن مِّن شَيْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ) [الإسراء : ٤٤] . فتبيَّنَ مِنْ قولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ وَلَكِن لَّا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ﴾ - ولم يقلْ ولكن لا تسمعونَ - أَنَّهُ تسبيحُ يُفْقَهُ بالقلوب الزكيَّةِ ذواتِ الأنظارِ السديدة ، ولا يُسمَعُ بالآذانِ . فقدِ استبانَ بما ذُكِرَ : أَنَّهُ جلَّ وعلا هو المحمودُ المُثْنى عليه بلسانِ الحال ، وبلسان المقال ، وبالجَنَانِ والأركانِ ، مع التعذُّرِ أنْ يفي ذلكَ كلُّهُ بالنَّزْرِ اليسير ممَّا لا يتناهى مِنْ صفاتِ الجلال والجمال . اللهمَّ؛ لا أحصي ثناءً عليكَ ، أنتَ كما أثنيتَ على نفسِكَ . هذا مما يتعلَّق بحمدِ الخَلْقِ وثنائهم عليهِ تعالى .
Kufran (ingratitude) indicates covering and concealment. There is no doubt that restricting gratitude to the heart contains a kind of concealment, except that this praise with the tongue, even if it has the honor that is known... it is a branch of the first, and by its originating from it it deserves honor, otherwise it would be hypocrisy. And this type of praise in the world is more general than what came before it. And this is indicated by His saying, exalted: 'And there is no thing but that it glorifies with His praise, but you do not understand their glorification' [al-Isra:44]. So it became clear from His saying, exalted: 'but you do not understand their glorification' – and He did not say 'but you do not hear' – that it is a glorification understood by pure hearts with sound insights, and not heard by ears. Misconception & Correction

What does Sanusi identify as the evidence for praise by states?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at yourself and your constant need for Allah—your breath, your heartbeat, your next meal— does your state really glorify Him, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What needs to change in your awareness today?
?
You just read that the entire universe is glorifying Allah, but you do not perceive it because your heart may not be pure enough. Will you ask Allah tonight to cleanse your heart so that you can join that cosmic tasbih with true understanding?
?
Sanusi ends with a confession: 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You.' Do you ever feel the weight of your inability to truly praise Allah? When you say SubhanAllah, are you content with that or does it drive you to seek a deeper, more constant state of praise in your daily life?

The Inadequacy of Creation's Praise
Sanusi explains the nature of creation's praise and cites a hadith on its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all creation praises Allah, but even combined praise cannot fully encompass His infinite attributes, as expressed in the Prophet's hadith.

Core Idea
All creation praises Allah, but none can praise Him adequately.

This passage from Sanusi builds on the previous discussion of cosmic praise. It clarifies that while everything in existence praises Allah—through state, speech, heart, and limbs—this praise remains infinitely insufficient. The Prophet's own admission of inability sets the standard for our understanding of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully. Notice how Sanusi contrasts what is heard by ears (which we cannot hear) with what is understood by pure hearts.
The phrase 'لا تسمعون' is key.
2
Identify the modes of praise
Find the four modes of praise mentioned: tongue of state, tongue of speech, heart, and limbs. Then note the conclusion: even all together cannot fulfill a little of the infinite attributes.
3
Connect to the hadith
Reflect on the Prophet's statement 'لا أحصي ثناءً عليك'. Why does Sanusi quote this hadith here? What does it teach about our relationship with praising Allah?
This hadith is a powerful reminder of divine transcendence.

ولكن لا تسمعون - أنه تسبيح يفقه بالقلوب الزكية ذوات الأنظار السديدة، ولا يسمع بالآذان. فقد استبان بما ذكر: أنه جل وعلا هو المحمود المثنى عليه بلسان الحال، وبلسان المقال، وبالجنان والأركان، مع التعذر أن يفي ذلك كله بالنزر اليسير مما لا يتناهى من صفات الجلال والجمال، اللهم؛ لا أحصي ثناءً عليك، أنت كما أثنيت على نفسك. هذا مما يتعلق بحمد الخلق وثنائهم عليه تعالى.

Commentary

Then it confirms that Allah is praised by all means: by the state (actions), speech, heart, and limbs. However, even all of that combined cannot adequately praise Him because His attributes are infinite. The author then cites the well-known hadith 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself' to emphasize the inadequacy of human praise. The passage concludes that this discussion concerns the praise of creation.

The Proof that God is Not a Body
Sanusi proves God is not a body using two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God is not a body, which is built on two premises: all bodies are contingent, and God is eternal.

Core Idea
The impossibility of God being a body follows from combining contingency of bodies with eternity of God.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a concise yet powerful proof that God cannot be a body. He relies on two established premises from earlier discussions: all bodies are necessarily contingent, and God is necessarily eternal. By combining these, the conclusion necessarily follows. This argument is a model for many similar proofs in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own logical flow come through.
Notice the two parts: the conclusion first, then the explanation.
2
Identify the two premises and conclusion
Find the statement that all bodies are contingent and the statement that God is eternal. See how they combine to yield the conclusion that God is not a body.
3
Understand the logical structure
Sanusi explains that the first premise gives the conditional premise, and the second gives the exceptive premise. Think about how a conditional syllogism works.
This proof is an example of a conditional syllogism (qiyas sharti).
4
Reflect on divine transcendence
Why is it important that God is not a body? Connect this to the broader theme of tanzih (divine transcendence) in Islamic theology.
This proof guards against anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنَّه جلَّ وعزّ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ حادثاً؛ لما تقرَّرَ مِنْ وجوبِ قِدَمِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ؛ فحينئذٍ ينتج له: أنَّ مولانا جلَّ وعزَّ يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ جِرْماً؛ فالمطلب الأوَّلُ الذي عرفناه بالبرهان - وهو وجوب الحدوثِ لجميع الأجرام - هو الذي قرَّرَ لنا الجزء الأَوَّلَ مِنْ هذا الدليل؛ وهو ملازمه الشرطية، والمطلب الثاني - وهو وجوبُ القِدَمِ له جلَّ وعلا ـ هو الذي قرَّرَ لنا الجزء الثاني مِنَ الدليل؛ وهو الاستثنائية، وقس على هذا، فمثله كثير، والله الموفق.
But He, may He be glorified and exalted, is impossible to be originated, because His eternity (qidam) is established as necessary; then from this it follows that our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, is impossible to be a body (jirm). So the first premise that we knew by demonstration — which is the necessity of contingency (huduth) for all bodies — is what established for us the first part of this proof; that is, the conditional premise. And the second premise — which is the necessity of eternity (qidam) for Him, may He be glorified and exalted — is what established for us the second part of the proof; that is, the exceptive premise. And analogize upon this, for similar cases are many, and God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that God is not a body. It is a classic kalam argument based on two premises. First, all bodies are necessarily contingent (hadith). Second, God is necessarily eternal (qadim). Combining these premises yields the conclusion that God cannot be a body, because if He were a body, He would be contingent, contradicting His necessary eternity. The author explains that the first premise (all bodies are contingent) provides the conditional premise of the proof, and the second premise (God is eternal) provides the exceptive premise. He then tells the reader to analogize this pattern for many similar arguments throughout the work.

Key Terms
جرم
حدوث
قدم
ملازمة شرطية
استثنائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this proof is circular because it assumes God is eternal, but the author has already established that through other proofs.
Correction: The proof is not circular; it simply applies the established premises of contingency of bodies and eternity of God. These premises are proven independently in earlier sections.

Checkpoint
What does the proof that God is not a body rely on?
A) The necessity of contingency for all bodies and the necessity of eternity for God
B) The possibility of bodies and the impossibility of God
C) The creation of the world and the attributes of God
D) The proof from motion and the proof from design

Key Takeaway
The proof that God is not a body is a direct application of the principles of contingency and eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If God were a body, He would be contingent—limited by time and change. Do you truly live as though God is beyond all limitations? Which of your worries reveals that you think God is too small or finite?
?
The proof rests on two premises you must accept. Have you let yourself off the hook by never fully affirming God's eternity with your heart? What would change in your daily life if you genuinely believed that God has no beginning and no end?

Divine Lights and the Seventy Veils
Sanusi describes divine lights in hearts and quotes the hadith of the seventy veils.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge is a light from Allah that removes veils of ignorance, and that direct unveiling of His glory would be overwhelming.

Core Idea
Allah’s lights tear the veils of ignorance.

Sanusi concludes by emphasizing that true knowledge of Allah is a light He places in the hearts of His servants. This light tears through the veils that prevent people from knowing the truth. He then quotes a profound hadith about the seventy veils of light, illustrating both the accessibility of divine guidance and the overwhelming nature of direct vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note key images
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the words 'lights' (anwar), 'veils' (hujub), and 'burning' (ihraq). These are central to the metaphor.
2
Understand the metaphor
The lights in hearts represent divine knowledge; the veils represent ignorance or barriers to knowing Allah. Tearing the veils means gaining true understanding.
3
Reflect on the hadith
The hadith says Allah has seventy veils of light. If removed, the glories of His Face would burn creation. This shows both His transcendence and the mercy of gradual revelation.
4
Consider the implications
We can know Allah through His lights (knowledge, guidance), but direct vision in this world is impossible because of our limited capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه أنوار الله تعالى في قلوب عبادِهِ ، وهذه هي خَرْقُ الحُجُبِ المانعة للمحجوبينَ عن الحقِّ ومعرفتِهِ ، « إِنَّ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى سَبْعِينَ حِجَاباً مِنْ نُورٍ ، لَوْ كَشَفَهَا لأَحْرَقَتْ سُبُحَاتُ وَجْهِهِ مَا أَنْتَهَى إِلَيْهِ بَصَرُهُ مِنْ خَلْقِهِ (۱) ، ومعناه : أنَّ اللهَ تعالى لو أطلعَ جميع عبادِهِ على العلمِ (۱) روى الطبراني في المعجم الأوسط ) ( ٦٤٠٧ ) من حديث سيدنا أنس رضي الله عنه مرفوعاً : «سألتُ جبريلَ عليهِ السلامُ : هل ترى ربَّكَ ؟ قال : وبينه سبعين حجاباً مِنْ نور ، لو رأيتُ أدناها لاحترقتُ » ، وروى مسلم ( ١٧٩ ) من حديث سيدنا أبي موسى رضي الله عنه مرفوعاً وفيه : « حجابه = إن بيني
So these are the lights of Allah in the hearts of His servants, and these are the tearing of the veils that prevent those veiled from the truth and knowledge of Him. 'Indeed Allah has seventy veils of light; if He were to remove them, the glories of His Face would burn whatever His sight reaches of His creation.' (1) And its meaning is that if Allah were to inform all His servants of knowledge... (1) Reported by al-Tabarani in al-Mu'jam al-Awsat (6407) from the hadith of our master Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, raised: 'I asked Gabriel, peace be upon him: Do you see your Lord? He said: Between Him and me are seventy veils of light; if I saw the nearest of them, I would be burned.' And Muslim reported (179) from the hadith of our master Abu Musa, may Allah be pleased with him, raised and in it: 'His veil is... between me...'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a concluding reflection on divine illumination. Sanusi states that the lights (anwar) of Allah in the hearts of His servants are the means of tearing the veils that prevent the veiled from accessing truth and knowledge of Him. He then quotes a hadith qudsi (or prophetic hadith) that Allah has seventy veils of light, and if they were removed, the glories of His Face would burn all that His sight reaches of creation. Sanusi then begins to explain that this means that if Allah were to reveal knowledge to all His servants... but the text cuts off. The footnote provides sources for the hadith from al-Tabarani and Muslim, giving alternative wordings. The passage emphasizes that true knowledge is a light from Allah that removes veils, but also that direct unveiling would be overwhelming.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the veils are physical or material barriers.
Correction: The veils are described as being of light (nur), indicating they are spiritual or divine manifestations. The passage shows they are not material, and knowledge comes gradually through illumination, not through direct unveiling.

B) The glories of His Face would burn all that His sight reaches of creation

Key Takeaway
The lights of Allah in the heart are the means to tear the veils of ignorance, yet direct unveiling of His glory would be overwhelming.

The Seventy Veils of Light
Sanusi cites and interprets the hadith of seventy veils of light.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the hadith of Allah's seventy veils of light and how it illustrates divine transcendence.

Core Idea
The veils of light protect creation from being overwhelmed by Allah's glory.

This lesson examines a profound hadith about Allah's seventy veils of light. The author explains that the lights in believers' hearts tear the veils that prevent people from knowing the truth. The hadith warns that if the veils were removed, the glory of Allah's face would burn everything. The interpretation reveals that this 'burning' is spiritual purification from attachment to creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its interpretation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first introduces the lights, then quotes the hadith, then explains its meaning.
The hadith is a well-known tradition, so focus on the author's interpretation.
2
Understand the metaphorical language
Reflect on what 'burning' means in this context. It is not physical but refers to the removal of all else from the heart.
This connects to the concept of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه أنوار الله تعالى في قلوب عبادِهِ ، وهذه هي خَرْقُ الحُجُبِ المانعة للمحجوبينَ عن الحقِّ ومعرفتِهِ ، « إِنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى سَبْعِينَ حِجَاباً مِنْ نُورٍ ، لَوْ كَشَفَهَا لأَحْرَقَتْ سُبُحَاتُ وَجْهِهِ مَا أَنْتَهَى إِلَيْهِ بَصَرُهُ مِنْ خَلْقِهِ (۱) ، ومعناه : أنَّ اللهَ تعالى لو أطلعَ جميع عبادِهِ على العلمِ بملكوتِ اللهِ تعالى وتنزيهه عن مشابهة خلقِهِ ، وتصرُّفِهِ فيهم . . لعلموا مِنْ تسبيح الله تعالى وتنزيهِهِ عن مشابهةِ خلقِهِ ما يحترق ويتلاشى به عن قلوبهم كلُّ ما سواه سبحانه وتعالى
So these are the lights of Allah Almighty in the hearts of His servants, and this is the tearing of the veils that prevent the veiled from the truth and knowledge of Him. 'Indeed, Allah Almighty has seventy veils of light; if He were to remove them, the glories of His Face would burn whatever His sight reaches of His creation' (1). And its meaning is: that if Allah Almighty were to inform all His servants of the knowledge of the dominion of Allah Almighty, His transcendence above resembling His creation, and His disposal of them ... they would learn from the glorification of Allah Almighty and His transcendence above resembling His creation that which would burn and obliterate from their hearts everything other than Him, glorified and exalted.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage presents a hadith about Allah's seventy veils of light and its interpretation. The author begins by stating that the lights mentioned previously are the lights of Allah in the hearts of His servants, and these lights constitute the tearing of the veils that prevent the veiled from the truth. He then quotes the hadith: 'Indeed, Allah Almighty has seventy veils of light; if He were to remove them, the glories of His Face would burn whatever His sight reaches of His creation.' Note the footnote marker (1) which typically refers to a source citation. After the hadith, the author provides its meaning: if Allah were to inform all His servants of the knowledge of His dominion (malakūt), His transcendence (tanzīh) above resembling His creation, and His disposal of them, they would learn from His glorification and transcendence that which would burn and obliterate from their hearts everything other than Him. The 'burning' here is metaphorical, referring to the removal of attachments to creation.

Correction: The veils are of light, and the 'burning' is a metaphor for spiritual purification—the obliteration of attachment to anything other than Allah.

First meaning of qidam: time-length impossible for Allah
Sanusi explains the first meaning of qidam as time-length and shows it is impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why describing Allah as 'qadim' in the sense of time-length is impossible, because time itself is a created contingent attribute.

Core Idea
Allah is beyond time; His pre-eternity is not old age.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a common misunderstanding about divine pre-eternity. Many people think of 'eternal' as extremely old – stretching back in time without end. But Sanusi shows that this concept of time-length cannot apply to Allah at all because time depends on motion and the spheres, which are created. The following passage defines this first meaning of qidam and demonstrates its impossibility for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of time-length
Read the passage and note how Sanusi defines 'time' in this context: it is measured by the motion of spheres, day and night, etc.
Pay attention to the phrase 'time in this sense' – it refers to measurable duration.
2
See why time did not exist in pre-eternity
Notice that before creation (al-azal), there were no spheres or motion, so time as we know it did not exist.
The Arabic emphasizes 'no sphere, no motion, no hour, no night, no day'.
3
Grasp why time cannot pass over Allah
Sanusi argues that time only applies to beings inside the world, subject to motion and change. Allah transcends all places and directions, so time cannot pass over Him.
The phrase 'imprisoned inside the world' illustrates dependence on location.
4
Conclude the correct meaning of qidam
The passage ends by distinguishing that qidam in this sense belongs only to contingent things, not to Allah.
This sets up the next meaning of qidam in later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجزائها ، وتعاقب الليل والنهارِ : ولا شك أنَّ الزمانَ بهذا المعنى منعدم أيضاً في الأزلِ ؛ إذْ لا فلكَ فيهِ ولا حركة ، فلا ساعة ولا ليل ولا نهار ، ويستحيل أن يمرَّ الزمانُ بهذا المعنى عليه جل وعزَّ ؛ لأنَّ الليل والنهار وأجزاء هما المصاحبة لحركاتِ الأفلاكِ . . إِنَّما تمرُّ على مَنْ سُجِنَ في داخل العالم ؛ بحيثُ تتحرَّكُ الأفلاكُ وكواكبها فوقَهُ وتحته ، ويمرُّ عليهِ بوساطتها بحسَب العادة الحر والبرد ، والصيف والشتاء ، ومَنْ تنزّه عن الأمكنة والجهات والتغيراتِ.. استحالَ أنْ يكونَ لهُ معَ شيءٍ مِنَ العالم اتصال أوِ انفصال فقدِ اتَّضِحَ لكَ : أنَّ القدمَ بهذا المعنى - أعني : باعتبار طولِ الزمان - خاص بالحوادثِ ، ويستحيل في حقه تعالى
And its parts, and the alternation of night and day: There is no doubt that time in this sense is also non-existent in pre-eternity (al-azal); for there is no sphere (falak) in it and no motion, so there is no hour, no night, no day. And it is impossible for time in this sense to pass over Him, may He be glorified and exalted; because night and day and their parts accompany the motions of the spheres. They only pass over one who is imprisoned inside the world, such that the spheres and their planets move above him and below him, and through them, by habit, heat and cold, summer and winter pass over him. And one who is transcendent beyond places, directions, and changes... it is impossible that with anything of the world he should have connection or separation. So it has become clear to you: that pre-eternity (al-qidam) in this sense – I mean, in consideration of length of time – is specific to contingent things (al-hawadith), and is impossible with respect to Him, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 237

Commentary
This passage defines the first meaning of qidam (pre-eternity) as the extension of time, measured by days, nights, and the motions of celestial spheres. Sanusi argues that such time did not exist in al-azal (pre-eternity before creation) because there were no spheres or motion. Therefore, this kind of time cannot pass over Allah, since time exists only within the physical universe. Only beings trapped inside the world experience the passage of time. Since Allah is utterly transcendent beyond places, directions, and changes, it is impossible for Him to have any connection or separation with anything in the world. The conclusion is that qidam in this sense belongs only to contingent things (al-hawadith) and cannot be attributed to Allah.

Key Terms
القدم (al-qidam)
الزمان (al-zaman)
الأزل (al-azal)
الحوادث (al-hawadith)
الفلك (al-falak)
الحركة (al-haraka)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Allah's eternity means He is extremely old in time, as if He existed for an unimaginably long duration.
Correction: This passage clarifies that time-based duration is impossible for Allah because time itself is a created phenomenon dependent on motion and spheres, which Allah transcends.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for Allah to be described as qadim in the sense of 'length of time'?
A) Because Allah is beyond time and motion
B) Because time is not real
C) Because time is a creation of Allah and He is not subject to it
D) Because Allah is eternal in the sense of having no beginning

Key Takeaway
Allah's pre-eternity is not defined by time-length because time itself is a created phenomenon dependent on motion and spheres, which Allah transcends.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

If time is a prison for those inside the world, examine your own life: how much of your anxiety, hurry, or regret stems from being enslaved by the clock? Will you carve out a moment today to consciously step back and remember that your Lord is not bound by minutes or hours?

The First Demand – Refuting Corporealism
Sanusi states the chapter topic and proves the first demand against corporealism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the Hashwiyya who attribute corporeality, direction, and place to Allah.

Core Idea
Allah is utterly unlike contingents – He is not a body.

Sanusi begins this chapter by establishing that Allah must be transcendent over all created attributes. The first demand refutes the Hashwiyya, a group that claimed Allah has a body, direction, and location. This passage lays the foundation for the doctrine of divine transcendence (mukhalafatu lil-hawadith).

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading and the first demand
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note how Sanusi frames the chapter with three demands, then focuses on the first.
The chapter title sets the overall topic.
2
Identify the refuted group and their beliefs
Find the name 'al-Hashwiyya' and the three attributes they wrongly affirm for Allah: corporeality, direction, and place.
3
Understand the significance of 'transcendence over contingents'
Ask yourself: why does Sanusi begin with refuting corporealism? Because it directly opposes the principle that Allah is unlike any created thing.
This connects to the learning objective.

باب الدلیل علی وجوب مخالفته تعالى للحوادث و عدم اتحاده بستره[؟]، و بیان الدليل على وجوب قیامه تعالی بنفسه ترجم لثلاثة مطالب : فإثبات المطلبِ الأَوَّلِ : يردُّ على الحشوية القائلين بالجسميَّةِ والجهة والمكان له ، تعالى الله عمَّا يقولُ الظالمون علوّاً كبيراً . وإثبات المطلب الثاني والثالث : يردُّ على النصارى والباطنية القائلين بجواز اتحادِهِ تعالى بغيره ؛ أي : يصير معه شيئاً واحداً ؛ كقول بعض النصارى باتحادِ اللاهوت بالناسوت ؛ أي : الإله بجسدِ عيسى عليه السلام ، وجعلَ بعضُهم الإله تعالى ليسَ ذاتاً يقومُ بنفسِهِ ، بل صفةً تقومُ بالغيرِ ، وادَّعى بعض النصارى ذلك في عيسى عليهِ السلامُ : أَنَّهُ قامَ بهِ الإلهُ قيام الصفة بالموصوف ، وادَّعى بعضُ الباطنيَّةِ مثلَ ذلكَ في أنفسهم ، تعالى الله عن قول المبطلين
Chapter on the proof of the obligation of His transcendence over contingents and His non-union with His veil[?], and the exposition of the proof of the obligation of His self-subsistence. He translated for three demands: The proof of the first demand: it refutes the Hashwiyya who affirm corporeality, direction, and place for Him — exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. And the proof of the second and third demands: it refutes the Christians and the Batiniyya who affirm the permissibility of His union with another, i.e., that He becomes one thing with it; like the saying of some Christians of the union of the divine (lahut) with the human (nasut), i.e., the deity with the body of Jesus, peace be upon him. And some of them made the Exalted God not a self-subsistent essence, but rather an attribute subsisting in another. And some Christians claimed that regarding Jesus, peace be upon him: that the deity indwelt him with the indwelling of an attribute in its subject. And some of the Batiniyya claimed similar regarding themselves. Exalted is Allah above the sayings of the falsifiers.

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter (bab) in which Sanusi sets out to prove two divine attributes: mukhalafatu lil-hawadith (transcendence over contingents) and qiyamuhu bi nafsihi (self-subsistence). The first is proven by refuting the Hashwiyya who say God is a body with direction and location. The second and third demands are combined to refute those who say God can unite with another (ittihad) or that He is an attribute subsisting in another (hulul). Sanusi specifies Christians who claim union of the divine with the human in Jesus, and also those (some Batiniyya) who claim similar for themselves. The passage emphasizes that God is not a self-subsistent essence but rather an attribute subsisting in another. The author ends with a declaration of divine transcendence over such falsehoods.

The Second and Third Demands – Refuting Union and Indwelling
Sanusi refutes union and indwelling, affirming divine self-subsistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the doctrines of union (ittihad) and indwelling (hulul) as claimed by some Christians and Batiniyya.

Core Idea
Allah is self-subsistent and cannot unite with or indwell any creation.

After establishing divine transcendence over corporeality, Sanusi moves to refute two related errors: that Allah can unite with another being (ittihad) or indwell a creation as an attribute (hulul). He targets Christians who claimed divinity united with Jesus, and Batiniyya who made similar claims. This passage affirms that Allah is self-subsistent (qiyamuhu bi nafsihi) and not an attribute subsisting in another.

Guided Path
1
Read the second and third demands together
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi treats these two demands as a single refutation.
The demands are linked because both involve making God non-self-subsistent.
2
Identify the two errors: union and indwelling
Find the description of union (ittihad) where God becomes one thing with another, and indwelling (hulul) where God is an attribute subsisting in a subject.
Union is exemplified by Christians claiming the divine united with Jesus; indwelling by claiming God dwelt in Jesus as an attribute.
3
Understand the consequence: Allah must be self-subsistent
Reflecting on these errors, Sanusi implies that if God were subject to union or indwelling, He would not be self-subsistent. Thus, proving qiyamuhu bi nafsihi refutes both.
This connects to the learning objective.

The Logical Refutation of Union (Ittihad)
Sanusi refutes the claim of union with God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that refutes the claim that God can be united with another being.

Core Idea
Union is impossible because two entities either remain two or do not remain existent.

This lesson presents Sanusi's concise logical refutation of the doctrine of ittihad (union or incarnation). He uses a disjunctive argument to show that no scenario allows two distinct things to become one. Understanding this argument reinforces the doctrine of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a series of 'if...then' statements.
The argument is built on a disjunction: either both remain existent or they do not.
2
Analyze the first branch
If both remain existent after union, they are still two, not one. So union fails.
3
Analyze the second branch
If they do not both remain existent, there are sub-cases: both cease and a third appears (not union); or one ceases and the other remains (the nonexistent cannot unite with the existent).
In all cases, union is impossible.
4
Connect to the broader doctrine
Understand why this argument is important: it defends God's oneness and transcendence against anthropomorphic or incarnational ideas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان القول بالاتحاد ولا متحدا بغيره؛ أي: يكون معه واحدا، وإلا فإن بقيا موجودين فهما بعد اثنان لا واحد، وإن لم يبقيا موجودين لم يتحدا أيضا؛ لأنه إن عدم كل منهما ووجد ثالث فظاهر، وإن عدم أحدهما وبقي الآخر فكذلك؛ لأن المعدوم لا يتحد بالموجود.
The invalidity of the claim of union. And He is not united with another; that is, He becomes one with it. Otherwise, if they both remain existent, then they are still two, not one. And if they do not both remain existent, they are also not united; because if each of them ceases to exist and a third comes into existence, it is apparent [that it is not union]; and if one of them ceases to exist and the other remains, it is likewise; because the nonexistent cannot unite with the existent.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the doctrine of ittihad (union or incarnation). The argument is a logical disjunction: if two things are united, either both remain existent or they do not. 1. If both remain existent, they are still two distinct things, not one. 2. If they do not both remain existent, then either (a) both cease to exist and a third appears (which is not union of the original two), or (b) one ceases and the other remains. In case (b), the nonexistent cannot unite with the existent, so union is impossible. Thus, union cannot occur under any logical scenario. The author concludes that God is not united with another (wa la mutahidan bi-ghairihi).

Key Terms
اتحاد
موجود
معدوم
واحد
اثنان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some sects claim that God can become one with a created being (e.g., incarnation).
Correction: The logical argument shows that no scenario allows two distinct things to become one. If both remain, they are still two; if one or both cease, union is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, what would happen if two things were united and both remained existent?
A) They become one
B) They are still two
C) They cease to exist
D) They become a third thing

Key Takeaway
Union of God with another being is rationally impossible in every logical scenario.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The logic shows that God cannot be united with anything. Yet do you ever, in your daily thoughts, act as if God is somehow mixed or joined with the world, or as if He is like something you know? How can you train your mind to always hold His absolute otherness?
?
If union is impossible, then any belief that reduces God to being part of creation is false. What beliefs or practices in your life might subtly imply that God is close in a way that compromises His transcendence? Will you examine them today?
?
The passage forces a choice: either God remains wholly distinct, or He ceases to be God. Do you truly live as if God is completely distinct and beyond comparison, or do you sometimes imagine Him in human terms? What will you change to ensure your worship reflects His uniqueness?

Why God Cannot Be a Body or Accident
Sanusi proves that the Creator of the world cannot be a body or an accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that the Creator must be free from bodily and accidental attributes, based on the nature of bodies and accidents as originated.

Core Idea
God's eternity excludes the possibility of being a body or accident.

Sanusi now applies the general principle that everything in the world—bodies and their accidents—is originated to the specific case of the Creator. He argues that if God were a body or an accident, He would be part of the world and thus subject to origination, which is impossible for the Eternal. This lesson works through that concise but powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the author's own words present the argument before you interpret it.
Notice the compact style: Sanusi states the conclusion first, then the proof.
2
Identify the two key premises
The proof relies on two premises: (1) all bodies and accidents are originated (ḥādith), and (2) God is pre-eternal (qadīm) and permanent (bāqī). Confirm these from the passage.
These premises have been established in earlier lessons.
3
Trace the logical argument
Follow the chain: If God were a body or accident → He would be part of the world → He would be originated (contradicting His eternity) → Therefore, it is impossible for God to be a body or accident.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming the opposite leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
محدِث العالم يستحيلُ أنْ يكونَ جرماً أو صفة للجرم (١) ويلزمُ أيضاً : أنْ يكونَ مُحدِثُ العالم ليسَ بِجِرْمٍ ولا صفةٍ للجرم ; لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوب الحدوث للأجرام وصفاتها يعني : لو كانَ تعالى جِرْماً أو عَرَضاً يقومُ بهِ لكَانَ مِنَ العالم ، وذلك يُؤدِّي إلى حدوثِهِ ; لما سبقَ لكَ مِنْ وجوبِ الحدوثِ للعالمِ كله(۱) ، وحدوثه تعالى محال ; لما عرفتَ مِنْ وجوبِ قدمِهِ جَلَّ وعزَّ (۲) ووجوب بقائه (٢) .
The Creator of the world cannot be a body or an accident (1). And it is also necessary that the Creator of the world is not a body nor an accident, because you know the necessariness of origination for bodies and their accidents. That is: if the Exalted were a body or an accident subsisting in something, He would be part of the world, and that would lead to His origination, due to what preceded for you of the necessariness of origination for the entire world (1), and His origination is impossible, due to what you know of the necessariness of His pre-eternity, may He be Glorified and Exalted (2), and the necessariness of His permanence (2).
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the Creator (muḥdith al-ʿālam) cannot be a body (jirm) or an accident (ṣifa li-l-jirm, i.e., an accident inhering in a body). Sanusi begins by stating the conclusion: it is impossible (mustaḥīl) for the Creator to be a body or an accident. He then gives the reason: bodies and their accidents are subject to origination (ḥudūth), and whatever is originated is part of the created world. If God were a body or an accident, He would be part of the world, and hence originated. But God's origination is impossible because we already know that He is necessarily pre-eternal (qadīm) and permanent (bāqī). The argument moves from the established premise that all bodies and accidents are originated to the conclusion that the Creator, being eternal, cannot be a body or accident. The author then clarifies: 'meaning: if the Exalted were a body or an accident subsisting in something, He would be part of the world, and that would lead to His origination.' The proof is straightforward and relies on the previously established classification of bodies and accidents as originated.

Key Terms
محدِث العالم
جرم
صفة للجرم
عرض
حدوث
قدم
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying that God is a body or accident implies that God does not exist or is nothing.
Correction: On the contrary, affirming that God is not a body or accident affirms His transcendence and perfection, as He is not limited by material composition or accidental attributes.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why cannot the Creator be a body or an accident?
A) Because bodies and accidents are contingent and God is necessary.
B) Because bodies and accidents are originated, and God is eternal.
C) Because bodies and accidents are physical and God is spiritual.
D) Because bodies and accidents are imperfect and God is perfect.

Key Takeaway
The Creator's eternity entails that He cannot be a body or accident, as both are originated and part of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you supplicate to Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him as having a form, location, or physical attributes? What specific mental image must you now strive to eliminate based on this lesson?
?
If Allah is neither a body nor an accident, then He is utterly unlike anything you have ever seen or experienced. How does this realization confront your tendency to conceptualize Him in familiar terms? What practical step can you take today to train your mind to think of His transcendence?
?
This lesson proves that the Creator must be eternal, while everything else is originated. Consider your own attachment to worldly things—your body, possessions, relationships. How does this truth recalibrate your priorities and remind you of what alone is worthy of ultimate love and fear?

The Refutation of Union (Ittihad): A Three-Case Logical Argument
Sanusi refutes the doctrine of union with a three-case logical proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the doctrine that God can unite with any other being, based on three exhaustive cases.

Core Idea
Union of two distinct existents is logically impossible in every scenario.

The doctrine of union (ittihad) claims that God could become one with another being. Sanusi refutes this by considering all logical possibilities for two things to become one. The argument is concise but comprehensive, showing that in every case, genuine union is impossible. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a claim, then a three-case argument, then a definition and restatement.
Pay attention to the logical flow from one case to the next.
2
Identify the three cases
The argument considers three possibilities: both things remain existing, both are annihilated, or one is annihilated and the other remains. Understand why each case fails to produce union.
These three cases exhaust all logical possibilities.
3
Grasp the foundational principle
Sanusi relies on the principle that each quiddity (māhiyya) necessarily excludes everything else. This makes union impossible.
This principle is key to the argument's validity.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Union is absolutely impossible in the eternal and the originated. This safeguards divine transcendence.
The conclusion applies universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بطلان القول بالاتحاد ولا مُتَحِداً بغيرِهِ ; أي : يكونُ معَهُ واحداً ، وإلا فإنْ بقيا موجودين فهما بعد اثنان لا واحد، وإن لم يبقيا موجودين لم يتَّحدا أيضاً ; لأنَّهُ إِنْ عُدِمَ كلٌّ منهما ووُجِدَ ثالثُ فظاهر ، وإِنْ عُدِمَ أحدهما وبقي الآخر فكذلكَ ; لأنَّ المعدومَ لا يَتَّحد بالموجودِ (۱) الاتحاد : عبارةٌ عن صيرورة شيئين شيئاً واحداً ، ولا يخفى أنَّهُ مستحيل مطلقاً في القديم والحادث وبرهانُهُ : ما ذكرَ في العقيدة : أنَّ أحد الشيئين إذا اتَّحد بالآخرِ - أي : صارَ معَهُ شيئاً واحداً ـ ; فإن بقيا موجودين على حالهما فهما بعد اثنان لا واحدٌ (۱) ، فلا اتِّحاد إذاً ; للقطع بأنَّ وجودَ أحدهما ليس عينَ الآخرِ ، ومِنَ المقرَّرِ : أَنَّهُ يلزمُ كلَّ ماهيَّة وجوب سلْبِ كلّ ما عداها عنها ، وإنْ عُدِما معاً كانَ الموجود غيرهما لا هما ، فلم يتحدا أيضاً ، وإنْ عُدِمَ أحدهما دونَ الآخر امتنع الاتحاد أيضاً ; لأنَّ المعدوم ليس عين الموجودِ ; لتنافيهما ، وهذا معنى قولي : ( لأنَّ المعدومَ لا يتَّحد بالموجودِ ( أي : لا يصير عين الموجودِ ) .
The refutation of the claim of union (ittihad). And He (God) is not united with another; that is, He is not one with it. Otherwise, if both remain existing, they are still two, not one. And if both do not remain existing, they also do not unite, because if each is annihilated and a third comes into existence, it is obvious; and if one is annihilated and the other remains, it is likewise, because the annihilated does not unite with the existing. (1) Union is an expression of two things becoming one thing. It is not hidden that it is absolutely impossible in the eternal and the originated. Its proof is what was mentioned in al-ʿAqīda: if one of the two things unites with the other – that is, becomes one with it – then if both remain existing in their state, they are still two, not one, so there is no union, because it is certain that the existence of one is not identical to the existence of the other. And it is established that every quiddity necessarily requires the negation of everything else from it. And if both are annihilated together, what exists is something other than them, not them, so they also do not unite. And if one is annihilated without the other, union is also impossible, because the annihilated is not identical to the existing, due to their mutual incompatibility. And this is the meaning of my statement: 'Because the annihilated does not unite with the existing' – that is, it does not become identical to the existing.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (bṭlān) of the doctrine of union (ittihād) – i.e., that God could become united with another being. Sanusi first states that God is not united with anything else. He then gives a concise logical argument by considering all logical possibilities for two things to become one. The argument proceeds in three cases: (1) If both things remain existing after the supposed union, they are still two, not one – so no union. (2) If both are annihilated and a third thing comes into existence, the third is neither of the original two – so no union. (3) If one is annihilated and the other remains, the annihilated cannot unite with the existing because the non-existent has no identity with the existent. The passage then provides the definition of union: 'two things becoming one thing' (ṣayrūrat shayʾayni shayʾan wāḥidan). Sanusi asserts that this is absolutely impossible (mustaḥīl muṭlaqan) whether in the eternal or the originated. The proof is repeated from his ʿAqīda: each quiddity (māhiyya) necessarily excludes everything else from itself, so two quiddities cannot become one. The three-case argument is the same: remaining existent, both ceased, or one ceased – all impossible. The passage ends by clarifying that the non-existent cannot become identical to the existent.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
قديم
حادث
ماهية
معدوم
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can become united with another being in a literal ontological sense.
Correction: Sanusi shows that union is logically impossible in all three cases, proving it is absolutely impossible.

Checkpoint
In the refutation of union, what happens if both things remain existing after the supposed union?
A) They become one thing.
B) They are still two, not one.
C) They are annihilated.
D) A third thing comes into existence.

Key Takeaway
Union of two distinct quiddities is impossible; thus God's transcendence is preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that your spiritual experiences of closeness to God might blur the boundary between you and Him? How does Sanusi's logical argument force you to reconsider that feeling and what does it demand from your worship—specifically, in how you express love and devotion without crossing into ontological confusion?
?
When you recite 'Laa ilaaha illa Allah', you affirm that nothing is worthy of worship except God. But do you truly believe that nothing—absolutely nothing—can be united with Him in essence? Or do you hold a hidden assumption that your own soul could, at some level, become 'one' with Him through mystical attainment? If Sanusi's proof is sound, what would that mean for the purity of your tawhid and the way you approach your connection with God?
?
One of the three cases in Sanusi's argument—the annihilation of one thing and the survival of another—seems to allow for a kind of 'absorption' where the lesser disappears into the greater. Yet Sanusi says even that is impossible. Is there an area of your life—a relationship, a career, a desire—that you have tried to 'merge' with your faith, thinking it could become part of your deen? How does this passage expose that as a logical error, and what must you do to ensure that nothing rivals God in your heart?

Proofs for the Impossibility of Direction for God
Sanusi proves the impossibility of God having a direction using multiple arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple rational arguments that demonstrate why Allah cannot be in any direction, based on the impossibility of direction being an attribute of the Eternal.

Core Idea
Direction implies bodily attributes; God is not a body, so direction is impossible.

Sanusi presents several interconnected arguments to establish that direction (jiha) is impossible for Allah. These arguments are grounded in the necessary relationship between direction and bodily properties. As you read, notice how each argument independently leads to the same conclusion: Allah transcends all spatial categories.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then follow the translation. Pay attention to the technical terms for directions and bodily organs.
2
Identify the thesis and each argument
The passage begins with a clear thesis. Then it lists several arguments. Try to list them in your own words.
3
Understand the logical connections
Notice how each argument links direction to a property of bodies (organs, space, motion, need for a specifier). Understand why that makes direction impossible for God.
This is the core of kalam reasoning.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
Sanusi notes that none of Ahl al-Sunnah affirm direction for God. Reflect on why this is important for Islamic creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ استحالة الجهة في حق القديم سبحانَهُ واستحاله أن يكون له جهةٌ ] وأنْ يكونَ ليسَ في جهةٍ مِنَ الجهاتِ ؛ لأَنَّهُ لا يعمرها إلا الأجرام، وألا تكونَ لهُ هو أيضاً جهةٌ ؛ لأنَّها مِنْ عوارض الجسمِ ؛ ففوقٌ مِنْ عوارض عضو الرأس ، وتحت مِنْ من عوارض عضوِ الرَّجُلِ ، ويمين من عوارض العضو الأيمن ، وشمال مِنْ عوارض العضو الأيسر ، وأمامٌ مِنْ عوارض البطن ، وخلفٌ مِنْ عوارضِ الظَّهِرِ ، وَمَنِ استحال عليهِ أنْ يكونَ جِرْماً .. استحالَ أَنْ يَتَصفَ بهذهِ الأعضاءِ ولوازمها على الضرورة . قوله : ( لأنَّهُ لا يعمرُها إلا الأجرام ) يعني : لأنَّ شغل الجهةِ يستلزم التحيز ، وكلُّ مُتحيّر فهو جزمٌ ، والله عزَّ وجلَّ يستحيل أن يكونَ جِرْماً ، ولأنَّهُ لو كانَ في جهةٍ لكانَ إِمَّا أكبر منها وإِمَّا أصغر (۱) ويحتاج إلى المخصص (۲) وأيضاً : فلو كانَ في جهةٍ لم يخلُ : إما أن يتحرَّكَ فيها أو يسكن ، وكلٌّ مِنَ الحركة والسكونِ حادث ، فما لا يخلو عنهما حادث وأيضاً : فلو كانَ في جهةٍ لاحتاجَ إِلى مَنْ يُخصِّصُهُ بجهةٍ دونَ جهة ، وذلك يستلزم الحدوث ولم يقل بالجهةِ أحدٌ مِنْ أهل السنَّةِ ، وإِنَّما قالَ بها طائفةٌ مِنَ (۱) سكت عن المساوي لحصول المقصود بدونه ، غير أنه إذا كانت الجهة في العرف واللغة هي الحيّز الذي يأخذه الجرم ويكون فيه ، ومن المعلوم أن المتحيز لا يكون أكبر من حيزه . . فهذا الترديد غير متصور ، ويجاب بأن الجهة لما كانت مستحيلة استحالت على كل تقدير على التقدير الذي لا يكون صفاقسي » ( ۲۱۹/۱ ) . (۲) لأن كل جهة لها جهة تقابلها ، فإذا حصل في أحد المتقابلين فلا بد له مخصص يخصه بها دون المقابلة. » صفاقسي » ( ۲۱۹/۱ ) .
[The impossibility of direction with respect to the Eternal, glory be to Him, and the impossibility of His having a direction] and that He is not in any of the directions; because only bodies occupy them, and also that He does not have a direction; because directions are accidents of the body: 'above' is an accident of the organ of the head, 'below' is an accident of the organ of the foot, 'right' is an accident of the right organ, 'left' is an accident of the left organ, 'front' is an accident of the belly, 'back' is an accident of the back. And for whom it is impossible to be a body, it is impossible for him to be characterized by these organs and their necessary concomitants, of necessity. His statement: 'Because only bodies occupy them' means: because occupying a direction entails being situated in space (tahayyuz), and everything situated in space is a body, and Allah, mighty and majestic, is impossible to be a body. And because if He were in a direction, He would either be greater than it or smaller (1), and would need a particularizer (2). Also: if He were in a direction, He would not be free from either moving in it or being still, and both motion and rest are originated, so that which is not free from them is originated. Also: if He were in a direction, He would need someone to specify Him with a direction rather than another, and that entails origination. And none of the People of the Sunnah have said (He is in) a direction; rather a group from (1) He omitted the equal because the purpose is achieved without it, except that when direction in custom and language is the space that the body occupies and is in, and it is known that what is situated in space is not greater than its space... then this alternative is inconceivable, and it is answered that since direction is impossible, it is impossible on every estimation, on the estimation that it is not... (Sfaxi, 1/219). (2) Because every direction has a direction opposite it, so if it occurs in one of the two opposites, it inevitably needs a particularizer to specify it with that direction rather than the opposite. (Sfaxi, 1/219).
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage begins with the thesis: the impossibility of direction (jiha) for God. The first argument: direction is an accident of bodily organs; since God cannot be a body, He cannot have direction. The author lists the six directions as accidents of specific body parts. The second argument: occupying a direction requires tahayyuz (being situated in space), which is a property of bodies; God is not a body. The third argument: if God were in a direction, He would be either larger or smaller than it, requiring a particularizer. The fourth argument: if in a direction, He would be subject to motion or rest, both originated, implying origination. The fifth argument: being in a direction requires a particularizer to choose that direction over its opposite, also implying origination. The passage concludes that none of the People of the Sunnah affirm direction for God; only a certain group (cut off) does. Footnotes from Sfaxi comment on the arguments.

الجهة
القديم
عوارض
الجسم
التحيز
الحركة
السكون
الحدوث
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe God is literally above or in a direction like a body.
Correction: The passage shows direction entails bodily accidents and spatial properties, which are impossible for God.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the arguments given in the passage to prove the impossibility of direction for God?
A) Direction requires occupying space (tahayyuz), which is a property of bodies
B) If in a direction, God would be subject to motion or rest, both originated
C) Direction is an accident of the body, and God is not a body
D) If in a direction, God would be composite (murakkab)

Key Takeaway
God is absolutely transcendent and free from direction, as direction implies bodily properties that are impossible for the Eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine God, do you catch yourself instinctively thinking of Him as being 'somewhere'? What does this lesson reveal about your subconscious assumptions? Take a moment to repent and renew your tawheed by affirming that He is not confined by space.
?
If God had a direction, He would be subject to change and need a creator. How does this knowledge affect your reliance on Him? Will you let this truth transform your worship from being about a distant entity to the absolutely independent, transcendent Lord?
?
The proof shows that even the concept of 'direction' is a created concept. Do you live as though God is limited by your understanding? Commit today to study more proofs of tanzih so that your awe of Him increases.

First Proof: Direction Entails Being a Body
Sanusi's first proof: direction implies body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first argument that occupying a direction necessarily implies being a body, which is impossible for God.

Core Idea
Direction implies body; God is not a body, so He has no direction.

Sanusi begins his case against God having a direction with a straightforward syllogism: anything that occupies a direction must occupy space, and occupying space means being a body. Since God cannot be a body, He cannot be in a direction. This first argument establishes a foundational principle of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage carefully once. Notice how Sanusi builds the argument in a cause-and-effect manner.
The translation may help clarify the logical connection.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms 'jiha' (direction), 'jirm' (body), and 'tahayyuz' (occupying space). Understand how they relate.
3
Trace the logical chain
Follow the argument: if in a direction → occupies space → is a body → impossible for God. Recognize that each step is necessary.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof is important for Islamic theology. It protects God's transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عوارض البطن ، وخلف مِنْ عوارضِ الظَّهِرِ ، وَمَنِ استحال عليهِ أنْ يكونَ جِرْماً .. استحالَ أَنْ يَتَصفَ بهذهِ الأعضاءِ ولوازمها على الضرورة . قوله : ( لأنَّهُ لا يعمرُها إلا الأجرام ) يعني : لأنَّ شغل الجهةِ يستلزم التحيز ، وكلُّ مُتحيّر فهو جزمٌ ، والله عزَّ وجلَّ يستحيل أن يكونَ جِرْماً ، ولأنَّهُ لو كانَ في جهةٍ لكانَ إِمَّا أكبر منها وإِمَّا أصغر (۱) ويحتاج إلى المخصص (۲) وأيضاً : فلو كانَ في جهةٍ لم يخلُ : إما أن يتحرَّكَ فيها أو يسكن ، وكلٌّ مِنَ الحركة والسكونِ حادث ، فما لا يخلو عنهما حادث وأيضاً : فلو كانَ في جهةٍ لاحتاجَ إِلى مَنْ يُخصِّصُهُ بجهةٍ دونَ جهة ، وذلك يستلزم الحدوث
The accidents of the belly, and the throat from the accidents of the back, and whoever it is impossible for him to be a body, it is impossible for him to be described with these organs and their necessary concomitants necessarily. His saying: (Because only bodies occupy them) means: because occupying direction necessitates occupying space, and everything that occupies space is a body, and Allah, exalted and magnificent, is impossible to be a body. And because if He were in a direction, He would either be larger than it or smaller (1) and would need a determinant (2). Also: if He were in a direction, it would not be free from either moving in it or being still, and both motion and rest are originated, so that which is not free from both is originated. Also: if He were in a direction, He would need one who specifies Him with a direction rather than another, and that necessitates origination.
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs that God cannot be in a direction. First, Sanusi argues that occupying a direction entails being a body (jism), and God cannot be a body. Second, if God were in a direction, He would either be larger or smaller than that direction, requiring a determinant (mukhassis) to specify one size, which implies origination. Third, if in a direction, God would be subject to motion or rest, both of which are originated (hadith); thus what cannot be free of them is originated. The conclusion is that direction (jiha) is impossible for God.

Key Terms
جهة (jiha)
جرم (jirm)
تحيز (tahayyuz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God being in a direction (e.g., above the heavens) is necessary for His transcendence.
Correction: The argument shows that direction implies body, so attributing direction to God would actually degrade His transcendence by making Him material.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's first argument, what does occupying a direction necessitate?
A) Being a body
B) Being eternal
C) Being omnipotent
D) Being beyond space

Key Takeaway
God cannot be in a direction because direction implies being a body, and God is not a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, even unconsciously, imagined Allah as being 'up there' in the sky or 'close' in a physical sense? This proof shows such mental images contradict His nature. How will you train your mind to think of Allah without any spatial concept?
?
The proof uses the fact that you and I are bodies, and we need directions. But Allah is not like us. In what areas of your worship or daily life have you (perhaps mistakenly) treated Allah as if He shares human limitations? What will you do to correct that?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah has no direction, no place, no body, then your reliance on Him becomes absolute—He is not confined to a mosque or a prayer mat. How does this truth change the way you seek Him at all times, not just in 'holy' places?
