First Proof: Distinguishing Attribute Implies Contingency
Sanusi presents the first proof for the impossibility of two gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's first proof that the existence of two gods leads to contradiction, using the concept of distinguishing attributes.

Core Idea
A distinguishing attribute between two gods would make both contingent, proving only One exists.

Sanusi now presents the first of two proofs for God's unity (tawḥīd). This proof shows that if there were two gods, each would require a distinguishing attribute, which would in turn make them contingent—contradicting their divinity. This argument is foundational for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key concept: distinguishing attribute
The argument hinges on the idea that if two gods existed, each must have a unique attribute that sets him apart from the other. Why can't this attribute be essential? Think about it.
3
Trace the logical steps
Follow the argument: attribute is accidental → contingent → requires a cause → originated → god depends on it → god is originated → contingent → impossible.
This is a typical kalam proof by contradiction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that both gods would be contingent, contradicting the definition of God as necessary. Therefore, only one necessary being can exist.
5
Reflect on the proof's place in the creed
Sanusi calls this 'the first proof among the two proofs of the creed.' It is a cornerstone of tawḥīd. Ask yourself how this argument strengthens faith in God's oneness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلزمَ إذاً أنْ تكونَ تلك الصفةُ المميّزة عارضةً لكلّ واحدٍ منهما جائزة له ، وذلك يستلزم حدوثها وافتقارها إلى الفاعل المخصص وإذا كانَتْ حادثةً لزمَ حدوث كلّ واحدٍ مِنَ الإلهين ؛ لاستحالةِ عُرُو كلّ واحدٍ منهما عن الصفة التي تُميّزه عن الآخرِ ، وقد وجبَ الحدوث لتلك الصفة التي ميَّزَتْهُ عن مثلِهِ ، فوجبَ حدوثه ؛ إِذْ ما لا يعرى عن الحادث حادث ضرورةً . هذا تقرير البرهان الأوَّلِ مِنْ برهاني العقيدة
Therefore, it necessarily follows that those distinguishing attributes are accidental to each one of them and contingent for him. And that necessitates their occurrence and their need for the specifying agent (al-fāʿil al-mukhaṣṣiṣ). And if they are contingent, then the occurrence of each one of the two gods is necessitated, due to the impossibility of each one of them being free from the attribute that distinguishes him from the other. And occurrence became necessary for that attribute which distinguished him from his like, so his occurrence became necessary; because whatever is not free from contingent things is necessarily contingent. This is the formulation of the first proof among the two proofs of the creed.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two proofs for God's unity (tawḥīd). The argument proceeds as follows: If there were two gods, each must have a distinguishing attribute (ṣifa mumayyiza) that sets him apart from the other. This attribute cannot be essential (since both share the same divine essence), so it must be accidental (ʿāriḍa) and contingent (jāʾiza). Every contingent thing requires a cause (fāʿil mukhaṣṣiṣ) to bring it into existence. Thus, the distinguishing attribute is originated (ḥāditha). But a being cannot exist without its distinguishing attribute; therefore, each god—depending on that attribute—must also be originated. Since anything never free from originated things is itself originated, both would be contingent. This contradicts the notion of a necessary being. Therefore, only one necessary God exists.

Key Terms
صفة مميزة
عارضة
جائزة
فاعل مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distinguishing attribute could be essential to each god, so no contingency arises.
Correction: Sanusi argues that since both gods share the same divine essence, the attribute that distinguishes them must be accidental, not essential. If it were essential, it would be shared, undermining distinction.

Checkpoint
What does the distinguishing attribute imply about each of the two supposed gods?
A) It is essential to each.
B) It is accidental and contingent, making each contingent.
C) It proves they are both necessary.
D) It shows they are independent of a cause.

Key Takeaway
The first proof demonstrates that positing two gods leads to logical contradiction because the attributes needed to distinguish them would make each originated and contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the logical certainty that only one God exists challenge any hidden assumptions you may hold about the ease of associating partners with Him in your daily life?
?
Do you truly believe that no other being shares any of God's attributes? When you rely on something other than God, are you not implicitly attributing a divine quality to it?
?
If every distinguishing attribute in creation points back to the One Necessary Being, what does the uniqueness of your own life and existence demand from you in response?

Second Proof: Mutual Hindrance and Refutation of Cooperation
Sanusi presents the second proof of mutual hindrance, including objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two gods leads to logical absurdities, whether they agree or disagree, and why cooperation does not solve the problem.

Core Idea
Two gods cannot exist because they would either cancel each other out or require a preference without reason.

The second proof of tawhid is a powerful logical argument that eliminates any possibility of multiple gods. Imam Sanusi shows that if two gods existed, any possible action would be subject to both their wills, leading to contradictions whether they agree or disagree. The proof also anticipates and refutes the objection that they could cooperate, showing that cooperation implies deficiency. This lesson walks through the intricate reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Start by reading the Arabic passage along with the translation. Notice the logical structure: assumption, consequence, and explanation.
The proof begins with 'if there were with Him a second...'
2
Analyze the three possibilities in agreement
The proof breaks down the case where two gods agree on an action into three impossible scenarios. Identify each scenario and why it fails.
Focus on the indivisible atom example.
3
Consider the objection and answer
The objection suggests cooperation as a way out. Read how Sanusi refutes it by pointing out that cooperation implies each god is not independently powerful.
Cooperation undermines the perfection required for divinity.
4
Reflect on the underlying principles
Notice the use of 'preference without a preferrer' and the equality of gods. These principles are key to the proof's strength.
The footnote explains why possibles are equally related to both gods.
5
Review the full argument's logical flow
Trace the entire argument: assumption → universal power → agreement/disagreement → impossibility → conclusion of tawhid.
This proof is considered one of the strongest rational proofs for God's unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البرهانُ الثاني : فتقريرُهُ : أَنَّهُ لو كانَ معَهُ تعالى ثانٍ مماثل لهُ في الألوهية .. لزمَ عجزهما معاً ، وذلك ينفي ألوهية كل واحدٍ منهما ، ويستلزم ألا يكونَ وُجد شيء مِنَ العَالَمِ ، ولا يُوجَدَ ممكنٌ مِنَ الممكنات ؛ لتوقف وجودها على وجود الإله القادر
وبيان لزوم عجزهما : أَنَّكَ عرفت فيما سبق أنَّ الإله يجب أَنْ تكون قدرته وإرادته عامتين في جميع الممكنات ، فإذا فُرِضَ وجود الإلهين مثلاً لزمَ أنَّهُ ما مِنْ ممكن يُوجَدُ إلا ويتعلق به قدرةُ كلّ واحدٍ منهما وإرادته ، وقدرة الإلهِ لا تكونُ إلا تامَّةً مستقلة ، فيلزمُ أَنْ يتمانعا على الفعل ، سواء اتفقا أو اختلفا
أَمَّا معَ الاتفاقِ : فلنفرض أنَّهما قصدا إلى إيجاد جوهر فرد مثلاً ، واتفقا على إيجادِهِ ، وقس عليهِ كلَّ ما لا يقبل الانقسام ، فلا يخلو حينئذ : إِمَّا أَنْ يقعَ ذلكَ الجوهر الفردُ لهما معاً ، وذلك محالٌ ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليه : إما انقسام ما لا يقبلُ الانقسام إنْ قُدْرَ أنَّ الذي أوجده كلُّ واحدٍ منهما غيرُ ما أوجدَهُ الآخرُ ! وهو لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذ الكلامُ إِنَّما هو في وجود الجوهر الفردِ ، وليسَ لهُ إلا وجود واحدٌ لا يمكن انقسامه ، وإما تحصيل ما قد حصلَ إِنْ قُدِّرَ أنَّ الذي أوجده كلُّ واحدٍ منهما هو عين ما أوجدَهُ الآخرُ ، وذلكَ يستلزمُ أَنْ يرجع الأثرانِ اللَّذانِ قُدِّرَ وقوعهما منهما أثراً واحداً ! وهو لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذْ كون الأكثر يكون عينَ الأقل واضح الاستحالة .
وإما أن يقع ذلك الجوهر الفردُ بأحدهما دون الآخر ، وهو محال ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ ترجيحُ أحدِ المتساويين بلا مُرجح (۱) ؛ لفَرْضِ تماثلهما ، ولأنَّهُ بالوجه الذي عجز أحدهما يجب عجز الآخرِ
فإن قلت : بقي احتمال ثالث في الاتفاقِ ، وليس في أدلَّتِكم ما يدفعه ؛ وهو أنْ يُدَّعى أنَّ مجموع الإلهين هما اللذانِ أوجدا ذلكَ الجوهر الفرد ، لا كلُّ واحدٍ منهما حتى يلزم تحصيل الحاصل وانقسام ما لا ينقسم، ولا أحدهما حتى يلزم الترجيح بلا مُرجح ، والعجز في أحدهما تحقيقاً ، وفي الآخرِ تقديراً ، وإنَّما نقولُ : هما كاثنين تعاونا على رفع شيء بحيثُ لا يستقلُّ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما برفع ذلكَ
(۱) في هامش ( هـ ) : ( لأن المقتضي للقادرية : ذات الإله ، وللمقدورية : إمكان الممكن ، فنسبة الممكنات إلى الإلهين المفروضين على السوية من غير رجحان ) .
As for the second proof: its formulation is that if there were with Him (Exalted) a second similar to Him in divinity, then both would be incapable together, and that negates the divinity of each one of them, and necessitates that nothing of the world would exist, nor any possible thing would come to be, because the existence of every possible depends on the existence of a powerful deity. Explanation of the necessity of their incapability: You have known previously that the deity must have His power and will encompassing all possibles. So if the existence of two gods is assumed, for example, then it necessarily follows that there is no possible that exists unless the power and will of each one of them are connected to it. And the power of a deity is only complete and independent, so it necessitates that they mutually hinder each other in action, whether they agree or disagree. As for the case of agreement: Let us suppose that they both intended to bring into existence an indivisible atom (jawhar fard), for example, and agreed on its existence, and analogize to that everything that does not accept division. Then there is no avoiding either that the indivisible atom occurs from both of them together – and that is impossible, because it necessitates either division of what does not accept division if it is supposed that what each one brought into existence is different from what the other brought into existence – and that is inconceivable, for the discussion is only about the existence of the indivisible atom, and it has only one existence that cannot be divided; or an obtaining of what has already been obtained if it is supposed that what each one brought into existence is identical to what the other brought into existence – and that necessitates that the two effects, which were supposed to occur from them, become a single effect! And that is inconceivable, because the greater being identical to the lesser is clearly impossible. Or that the indivisible atom occurs from one of them without the other – and that is impossible, because it necessitates a preference of one of two equals without a preferrer, due to their assumed similarity, and because in the same way that one is incapable, the other must be incapable. If you say: There remains a third possibility in the case of agreement, and your evidence does not refute it: which is that it could be claimed that the sum of the two gods together brought about that indivisible atom, not each one individually such that the problems of obtaining the obtained and division of the indivisible occur, nor one of them such that preference without a preferrer and actual incapability of one and potential incapability of the other occur. Rather, we say: They are like two persons who cooperate to lift something, such that each one individually is not independent in lifting it. (Footnote in margin (H): Because the determinant of ability is the essence of the deity, and of the possible is the possibility of the possible. So the relation of possibles to the supposed two gods is on a par without preference.)
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for God's unity, known as the argument from mutual hindrance (tamānuʿ). The proof proceeds: 1) If two gods exist, each must have complete and independent power over all possibles. 2) For any possible act, both powers would pertain to it. 3) If they agree on an act (e.g., creating an indivisible atom), three possibilities arise: (a) The act occurs from both together – impossible because it would either split the indivisible (if differences exist) or multiply the same effect (if identical). (b) The act occurs from one only – requires a preference without a preferrer, impossible, and both are equally incapable. 4) If they disagree, one wills existence, the other non-existence, resulting in mutual hindrance and no act occurs. Thus, both would be incapable, contradicting divinity. An objection suggests cooperation: the two gods together produce the effect without each acting independently. The answer refutes this: cooperation implies each alone is insufficient, hence deficient, which is inconsistent with divine perfection. The footnote explains that the ability of a deity is determined by essence, and possibles are equally related to both, so no preference exists. This solidifies the impossibility of two gods.

Key Terms
تمانع
جوهر فرد
ترجيح بلا مرجح
تماثل
تعاون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that two gods could cooperate to create the world, avoiding mutual hindrance.
Correction: Cooperation implies each god alone is insufficient, which contradicts the perfection and independence required for divinity. The proof shows that even if they cooperate, each lacks independent power, so they are not truly divine.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why is it impossible for two gods to agree on creating an indivisible atom?
A) They would both be incapable.
B) It leads to either dividing the indivisible or duplicating a single effect, both absurd.
C) They could cooperate without issue.
D) They would disagree and fail.

Key Takeaway
The second proof eliminates multiple gods by showing that any pair of gods leads to logical contradictions, whether they agree or disagree, and cooperation does not rescue the assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that any 'partnership' in divinity leads to mutual ruin. Is there any area of your life where you are depending on something or someone else as if it has independent power alongside Allah? What is one practical step you can take today to transfer that trust wholly to Him?
?
If two gods cannot coexist, then your heart cannot be divided between Allah and any other ultimate concern. What worldly attachment currently competes for the throne of your heart? How will you demote it today?
?
The objection of cooperation is refuted because it implies each god is not perfect alone. Are there any 'gods' in your life that you rely on as supplements to Allah, thinking they complete what He lacks? Confess that lie and commit to seeing Allah as completely sufficient.

The Impossibility of Two Gods Creating an Indivisible Atom
Sanusi presents a disjunctive argument against two gods creating a single atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is logically impossible for two gods to jointly create a single indivisible atom, given the constraints of divine power and the nature of indivisible substance.

Core Idea
Two creators for one atom leads to either division of the indivisible or duplication of the existent.

In this passage, Sanusi deploys a disjunctive argument to rule out any scenario in which two divine beings could cooperate to produce a single atom. He first considers two broad possibilities: either both create the atom together, or only one does. Each path is shown to entail an absurdity. This establishes a foundational argument for divine unity in action.

Guided Path
1
Read the disjunctive argument
Read the first part of the passage where Sanusi presents the two main possibilities (the atom from both together, or from one alone). Note how he breaks each into sub-possibilities.
The argument is a 'proof by cases'.
2
Examine the first case: both together
Notice the two subcases: either each god creates a different part (leading to division of the indivisible) or they create the same existent (leading to 'obtaining the already obtained'). Both are absurd.
These are the two impossible outcomes under the first possibility.
3
Examine the second case: one alone
Understand why preponderance without a preponderator is impossible when both gods are equal in power. The equality assumption forces either both be capable or both incapable; if one acts, the other must be incapable, violating equality.
The concept of 'murajjiḥ' (preponderator) is crucial.
4
Summarize the disjunctive proof
Reflect on how these two possibilities exhaust the logical space, and both lead to impossibility. Therefore, the initial assumption of two gods creating an atom is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِمَّا أَنْ يقعَ ذلكَ الجوهر الفردُ لهما معاً ، وذلك محالٌ ؛ لأنَّهُ يلزمُ عليه : إما انقسام ما لا يقبلُ الانقسام إنْ قُدْرَ أنَّ الذي أوجده كلُّ واحدٍ منهما غيرُ ما أوجدَهُ الآخرُ ! وهو لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذ الكلامُ إِنَّما هو في وجود الجوهر الفردِ ، وليسَ لهُ إلا وجود واحدٌ لا يمكن انقسامه ، وإما تحصيل ما قد حصلَ إِنْ قُدِّرَ أنَّ الذي أوجده كلُّ واحدٍ منهما هو عين ما أوجدَهُ الآخرُ ، وذلكَ يستلزمُ أَنْ يرجع الأثرانِ اللَّذانِ قُدِّرَ وقوعهما منهما أثراً واحداً ! وهو لا يُعقَلُ ؛ إذْ كون الأكثر يكون عينَ الأقل واضح الاستحالة .
وإما أن يقع ذلك الجوهر الفردُ بأحدهما دون الآخر ، وهو محال ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ ترجيحُ أحدِ المتساويين بلا مُرجح (١) ؛ لفَرْضِ تماثلهما ، ولأنَّهُ بالوجه الذي عجز أحدهما يجب عجز الآخرِ .
فإن قلت : بقي احتمال ثالث في الاتفاقِ ، وليس في أدلَّتِكم ما يدفعه ؛ وهو أنْ يُدَّعى أنَّ مجموع الإلهين هما اللذانِ أوجدا ذلكَ الجوهر الفرد ، لا كلُّ واحدٍ منهما حتى يلزم تحصيل الحاصل وانقسام ما لا ينقسم، ولا أحدهما حتى يلزم الترجيح بلا مُرجح ، والعجز في أحدهما تحقيقاً ، وفي الآخرِ تقديراً ، وإنَّما نقولُ : هما كاثنين تعاونا على رفع شيء بحيثُ لا يستقلُّ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما برفع ذلكَ الشيء ، وإنما يتأتي الرفع منهما مجتمعين .
قلتُ : لا خفاءَ في استحالة هذا الاحتمال ، وتناول الدليل له ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ القدرة الضعيفة لكلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الإلهين في هذا الفَرْضِ لا تخلو عندَ فَرْضِ اجتماعِهما على إيجادِ الجوهرِ مِنْ أَنْ يُقالَ : هل كانَ لها (١) شيء مِنَ التأثير أو لا ؟ فإن كان لها شيءٌ مِنَ التأثير لم يخلُ : إمَّا أن يكون أثر إحداهما هو عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه تحصيل الحاصل كما سبق في الإلهين المستقلين ، أو يكون أثر إحداهما ليس عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه كونُ الأثر الواحد - الذي هو وجود الجوهر الفرد - يرجع أثرينِ ، وهو معنى ما ألزمنا قبلُ مِنْ انقسامِ ما لا ينقسم . هذا كلُّهُ إنْ فُرضَ أنَّ لكلَّ مِنَ القدرتين شيئاً مِنَ التأثير عند اثنين (٢) . وأمَّا إِنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ لا تأثير لكلِّ منهما في حال الاجتماع : فإنَّه يلزمُ حينَئِذٍ أَنَّ مجموعهما لا أثرَ لهُ ؛ إذ الصفةُ غيرُ المؤثرَةِ إِذا جُمِعَتْ إِلَى صفة أخرى غير مُؤثرةٍ ؛ كعلم مثلاً ضُمَّ إلى علم آخر.. لم يكن
Either that indivisible atom occurs from both of them together – and that is impossible, because there would follow from it: either division of what does not accept division, if it is supposed that what each one of them created is other than what the other created – and that is inconceivable, for the discussion is only about the existence of the indivisible atom, and it has only one existence that cannot be divided; or obtaining what has already been obtained if it is supposed that what each one of them created is exactly what the other created, and that entails that the two effects, which were supposed to occur from them, become one effect – and that is inconceivable, for the more being identical to the less is clearly impossible. Or that indivisible atom occurs from one of them to the exclusion of the other – and that is impossible, because there would follow from it the preponderance of one of the two equals without a preponderator (1), due to the assumption of their similarity, and because by the aspect by which one is incapable, the other must be incapable. If you say: A third possibility remains in the agreement, and there is nothing in your proof to refute it – namely, that it be claimed that the sum of the two gods is what created that indivisible atom, not each one of them individually such that obtaining the already obtained and division of what does not divide would follow, nor one of them such that preponderance without a preponderator would follow, and incapacity in one of them actually and in the other hypothetically; rather we say: They are like two who cooperate to lift something such that each one of them alone is not independent to lift that thing, and the lifting occurs only from them together. I say: There is no concealment of the impossibility of this possibility, and the proof encompasses it. That is because the weak power of each of the two gods in this hypothesis, when supposing their coming together to create the atom, is not free from being said: Did it have any effect or not? If it had some effect, it could not be free from either: the effect of one being exactly the effect of the other, so obtaining the already obtained would follow as before in the case of the independent gods; or the effect of one is not exactly the effect of the other, so there would follow from it that the single effect – which is the existence of the indivisible atom – becomes two effects, and that is the meaning of what we required before of division of what does not divide. All this if it is supposed that each of the two powers has some effect when there are two (2). But if it is supposed that there is no effect for each of them in the state of coming together, then there would follow that their aggregate has no effect; for a non-effective attribute when added to another non-effective attribute – like knowledge, for example, added to another knowledge – does not become [effective].
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a classical kalam refutation of the possibility of two gods (or divine beings) creating a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard). The argument is a disjunctive proof that covers all possibilities and shows each leads to an absurdity. First, the author considers two initial possibilities: either both gods create the atom together (معاً), or only one of them creates it. For the first case (both together), two sub-possibilities arise: either each god creates a part of the atom, leading to division of what is indivisible, or each creates the very same existent, which is 'obtaining what has already been obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل), i.e., an effect already actualized by the other. Both are impossible. For the second case (one god alone), the problem is that since the two gods are assumed equal in power (متساويان), there is no preponderator (مرجح) to prefer one over the other to be the sole creator; otherwise, the other would be incapable in the very aspect the first is capable, which contradicts equality. The author then presents an objection: the critic proposes a third possibility not covered—that the two gods together, as a collective agent, create the atom, not each individually. In this scenario, each has insufficient power alone, but combined they succeed, like two people lifting a heavy object together. The author answers by analyzing the nature of the proposed 'weak power' (قدرة ضعيفة) of each god. When they act together, either each one's power has some effect (تأثير) on the atom or it has no effect at all. If each has some effect, then either the effects are identical (leading again to تحصيل الحاصل) or they are distinct (leading to the indivisible atom having two effects, which requires it to be divisible—absurd). If neither has any effect in the combined state, then the aggregate of two non-effective attributes is also non-effective, analogous to adding two bits of knowledge that remain inert. Thus the third possibility collapses into the same impossibilities. The argument thus exhaustively demonstrates the logical necessity of divine unity (tawhid) by showing that multiple creators cannot coherently produce even a single atom.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
تحصيل الحاصل
مرجح
تماثل

Checkpoint
In Sanusi's argument against two gods creating an indivisible atom, what absurdity arises if each god individually creates a different part of the atom?
A) The atom would be divisible.
B) The act would be redundant.
C) The gods would be unequal.
D) The atom would have two creators.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that the concept of two creators for a single indivisible atom leads to logical contradictions, reinforcing that divine action is uniquely singular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If this argument shows that not even a single atom can be created by two, what does that mean for the moments in your life where you act as if you have partners with Allah in control? Where in your heart do you still harbor the belief that something else shares in creating your outcomes?
?
The argument hinges on the impossibility of division or duplication. In your worship, are you dividing your intention between Allah and others, or duplicating your devotion to multiple objects? Where have you failed to make Allah the sole focus of your effort?
?
If the equality of two gods leads to chaos in creation, what about the 'gods' you have set up in your life—your desires, your status, your plans? Do you see how they cannot truly share in Allah's exclusive lordship? What would it cost you to remove them from the throne of your heart?

Answering the Objection of Cooperative Agency
Sanusi answers the objection of cooperative agency by analyzing the effect of each power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi shows that even if two gods cooperate with limited individual power, the logical problems of creating a single atom persist.

Core Idea
Cooperative agency still forces either identity or division of effects.

Having presented the initial disjunctive proof, Sanusi anticipates an objection: what if the two gods are not each independently powerful but rather cooperate like two people lifting a heavy object? In this response, he analyzes the nature of each god's power and demonstrates that even this scenario collapses into the same absurdities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection claims a third possibility: the two gods together (collectively) create the atom, each having insufficient power alone but sufficient together. Understand this proposal.
This is analogous to two people lifting a stone.
2
Follow Sanusi's analysis of the weak power
Sanusi asks: does each god's power have any effect when they act together? He considers two scenarios: each has some effect, or each has no effect.
This is a crucial bifurcation.
3
If each has some effect...
Then either the effects are identical (leading to tahsīl al-ḥāṣil) or distinct (leading to the single atom having two effects, implying division of the indivisible).
Same problems as before.
4
If neither has any effect...
Then the combined powers also have no effect, since two non-effective attributes cannot produce an effect. The example of knowledge is given.
This refutes the cooperative model.
5
Conclude the refutation
All possibilities lead to absurdity, so the objection fails. The proof of divine unity is complete.

Source Text

وإما أن يقع ذلك الجوهر الفردُ بأحدهما دون الآخر ، وهو محال ؛ لأنَّه يلزمُ عليهِ ترجيحُ أحدِ المتساويين بلا مُرجح (١) ؛ لفَرْضِ تماثلهما ، ولأنَّهُ بالوجه الذي عجز أحدهما يجب عجز الآخرِ .
فإن قلت : بقي احتمال ثالث في الاتفاقِ ، وليس في أدلَّتِكم ما يدفعه ؛ وهو أنْ يُدَّعى أنَّ مجموع الإلهين هما اللذانِ أوجدا ذلكَ الجوهر الفرد ، لا كلُّ واحدٍ منهما حتى يلزم تحصيل الحاصل وانقسام ما لا ينقسم، ولا أحدهما حتى يلزم الترجيح بلا مُرجح ، والعجز في أحدهما تحقيقاً ، وفي الآخرِ تقديراً ، وإنَّما نقولُ : هما كاثنين تعاونا على رفع شيء بحيثُ لا يستقلُّ كلُّ واحدٍ منهما برفع ذلكَ الشيء ، وإنما يتأتي الرفع منهما مجتمعين .
قلتُ : لا خفاءَ في استحالة هذا الاحتمال ، وتناول الدليل له ؛ وذلكَ أنَّ القدرة الضعيفة لكلِّ واحدٍ مِنَ الإلهين في هذا الفَرْضِ لا تخلو عندَ فَرْضِ اجتماعِهما على إيجادِ الجوهرِ مِنْ أَنْ يُقالَ : هل كانَ لها (١) شيء مِنَ التأثير أو لا ؟ فإن كان لها شيءٌ مِنَ التأثير لم يخلُ : إمَّا أن يكون أثر إحداهما هو عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه تحصيل الحاصل كما سبق في الإلهين المستقلين ، أو يكون أثر إحداهما ليس عين أثر الأخرى ، فيلزم عليه كونُ الأثر الواحد - الذي هو وجود الجوهر الفرد - يرجع أثرينِ ، وهو معنى ما ألزمنا قبلُ مِنْ انقسامِ ما لا ينقسم . هذا كلُّهُ إنْ فُرضَ أنَّ لكلَّ مِنَ القدرتين شيئاً مِنَ التأثير عند اثنين (٢) . وأمَّا إِنْ فُرِضَ أَنَّهُ لا تأثير لكلِّ منهما في حال الاجتماع : فإنَّه يلزمُ حينَئِذٍ أَنَّ مجموعهما لا أثرَ لهُ ؛ إذ الصفةُ غيرُ المؤثرَةِ إِذا جُمِعَتْ إِلَى صفة أخرى غير مُؤثرةٍ ؛ كعلم مثلاً ضُمَّ إلى علم آخر.. لم يكن
Either that indivisible atom occurs from both of them together – and that is impossible, because there would follow from it: either division of what does not accept division, if it is supposed that what each one of them created is other than what the other created – and that is inconceivable, for the discussion is only about the existence of the indivisible atom, and it has only one existence that cannot be divided; or obtaining what has already been obtained if it is supposed that what each one of them created is exactly what the other created, and that entails that the two effects, which were supposed to occur from them, become one effect – and that is inconceivable, for the more being identical to the less is clearly impossible. Or that indivisible atom occurs from one of them to the exclusion of the other – and that is impossible, because there would follow from it the preponderance of one of the two equals without a preponderator (1), due to the assumption of their similarity, and because by the aspect by which one is incapable, the other must be incapable. If you say: A third possibility remains in the agreement, and there is nothing in your proof to refute it – namely, that it be claimed that the sum of the two gods is what created that indivisible atom, not each one of them individually such that obtaining the already obtained and division of what does not divide would follow, nor one of them such that preponderance without a preponderator would follow, and incapacity in one of them actually and in the other hypothetically; rather we say: They are like two who cooperate to lift something such that each one of them alone is not independent to lift that thing, and the lifting occurs only from them together. I say: There is no concealment of the impossibility of this possibility, and the proof encompasses it. That is because the weak power of each of the two gods in this hypothesis, when supposing their coming together to create the atom, is not free from being said: Did it have any effect or not? If it had some effect, it could not be free from either: the effect of one being exactly the effect of the other, so obtaining the already obtained would follow as before in the case of the independent gods; or the effect of one is not exactly the effect of the other, so there would follow from it that the single effect – which is the existence of the indivisible atom – becomes two effects, and that is the meaning of what we required before of division of what does not divide. All this if it is supposed that each of the two powers has some effect when there are two (2). But if it is supposed that there is no effect for each of them in the state of coming together, then there would follow that their aggregate has no effect; for a non-effective attribute when added to another non-effective attribute – like knowledge, for example, added to another knowledge – does not become [effective].
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage presents a classical kalam refutation of the possibility of two gods (or divine beings) creating a single indivisible atom (jawhar fard). The argument is a disjunctive proof that covers all possibilities and shows each leads to an absurdity. First, the author considers two initial possibilities: either both gods create the atom together (معاً), or only one of them creates it. For the first case (both together), two sub-possibilities arise: either each god creates a part of the atom, leading to division of what is indivisible, or each creates the very same existent, which is 'obtaining what has already been obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل), i.e., an effect already actualized by the other. Both are impossible. For the second case (one god alone), the problem is that since the two gods are assumed equal in power (متساويان), there is no preponderator (مرجح) to prefer one over the other to be the sole creator; otherwise, the other would be incapable in the very aspect the first is capable, which contradicts equality. The author then presents an objection: the critic proposes a third possibility not covered—that the two gods together, as a collective agent, create the atom, not each individually. In this scenario, each has insufficient power alone, but combined they succeed, like two people lifting a heavy object together. The author answers by analyzing the nature of the proposed 'weak power' (قدرة ضعيفة) of each god. When they act together, either each one's power has some effect (تأثير) on the atom or it has no effect at all. If each has some effect, then either the effects are identical (leading again to تحصيل الحاصل) or they are distinct (leading to the indivisible atom having two effects, which requires it to be divisible—absurd). If neither has any effect in the combined state, then the aggregate of two non-effective attributes is also non-effective, analogous to adding two bits of knowledge that remain inert. Thus the third possibility collapses into the same impossibilities. The argument thus exhaustively demonstrates the logical necessity of divine unity (tawhid) by showing that multiple creators cannot coherently produce even a single atom.

Key Terms
قدرة ضعيفة
تأثير
تحصيل الحاصل
انقسام ما لا ينقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two gods cooperate with limited individual power, they can create an atom without contradiction.
Correction: Sanusi shows that even in cooperation, the individual powers must either have some effect (leading to duplication or division) or no effect (making the aggregate ineffective), both of which are impossible.

Checkpoint
In Sanusi's response to the cooperative agency objection, what follows if each of the two gods' powers has no effect at all?
A) The aggregate cannot create the atom.
B) The atom would have two effects.
C) The gods would be unequal.
D) The proof fails.

Key Takeaway
Even if gods cooperate with limited power, the creation of a single atom still leads to logical contradictions, reaffirming the necessity of a single creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on created means as if they have independent power, forgetting that only Allah's power is truly effective? How can you train your heart to see all causes as merely instruments of His will?
?
When you encounter logical proofs for tawhid like this one, does it stir a deeper conviction in your heart, or do you treat it as mere intellectual exercise? What would change if you truly internalized that any co-creator is logically impossible?
?
The objector's suggestion sounds plausible—'they cooperate like two people lifting a stone.' Have you ever justified a subtle form of shirk in your actions or thoughts by claiming it's just 'cooperation'? Will you commit to examining your intentions tonight before sleep?

Impossibility of Composing God from Separate Essences
Sanusi refutes composition from two separate essences via a fortiori argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that God cannot be composed of two separate essences, and how this leads to division of attributes.

Core Idea
A composite God would have divided attributes—impossible for the One.

Sanusi anticipates a second objection: perhaps the combined entity of two weak gods is the true God. He refutes this by showing it leads to composition from separate essences, which is rationally inconceivable. This lesson follows his a fortiori argument and its consequence for divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the hypothetical claim that the combined entity is God.
The passage uses a condensed style; take your time.
2
Identify the refutation
Focus on the a fortiori argument: if composition from connected essences is impossible, then from separate essences is even more absurd.
This is a key logical move in kalām.
3
Trace the consequence
Understand that composition leads to each essence having a part of power and will—division of the attribute (tajziʾa al-maʿnā), which is impossible for God.
This shows why divine simplicity is necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن زعم المقدِّرُ أنَّ مجموعهما هو الإله ، لا كلَّ واحدٍ منهما . . كان ذلك تركيباً للإلهِ مِنْ ذاتين منفصلتين ، وكلُّ هذا ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ لعاقل ، وإذا استحال تركيب الإلهِ مِنْ ذاتين متصلتين فكيف يتركَّبُ مِنْ ذاتين منفصلتين ؟! ويؤدي ذلك أيضاً إلى أن يكونَ القائم بكلِّ واحدٍ منهما بعضَ القدرةِ وبعضَ الإرادةِ ، وذلك تجزئة للمعنى ! وهو ظاهر الاستحالة .
And if the [hypothetical] positer claims that their combined entity is God, not each one of them individually—that would be a composition of God from two separate essences, and all of this is inconceivable to any rational person. And if composition of God from two connected essences is impossible, how can He be composed from two separate essences?! And that also leads to that which subsists in each one of them being a part of power and a part of will, and that is a division of the attribute (maʿnā)! And that is manifestly impossible.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer within the larger refutation. The hypothetical opponent (al-muqaddir) might claim that it is not the individual weak gods that are divine, but rather their combined entity. Sanusi refutes this by pointing out that this would entail God being composed (tarkīb) of two separate essences (dhātayn munfaṣilatayn). He argues that this is rationally inconceivable (lā yuʿqal li-ʿāqil). He then uses an a fortiori argument: if composition from two connected essences is already impossible for God (as established in kalām regarding divine simplicity), then composition from separate essences is even more absurd.

Furthermore, Sanusi notes that if God were composed of two essences, each essence would possess only a part of power (qudra) and a part of will (irāda). This would mean dividing the divine attributes (tajziʾa al-maʿnā)—i.e., the attributes would be fragmented, which is manifestly impossible for the absolutely simple and indivisible God.

Key Terms
تركيب
ذات
تجزئة
قدرة
إرادة
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God could be composed of parts while still being one.
Correction: Sanusi shows that composition from separate essences necessarily divides the divine attributes, which is incompatible with perfect unity.

Checkpoint
What does Sanusi argue would follow if God were composed of two separate essences?
A) Each essence would have a part of power and will.
B) The essences would be equal in power and will.
C) God would be stronger than before.
D) The essences would be able to act independently.

Key Takeaway
Composing God from separate essences is impossible because it entails dividing His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power and will cannot be divided into parts, then in what areas of your life have you been 'dividing' your reliance on God—trusting Him partially and leaning on other things for the rest? How does this truth about His absolute unity call you to repent from subtle shirk in your heart and actions?
?
Sanusi shows that combining two separate essences leads to each having only a portion of divine attributes. Do you ever treat God's mercy or justice as if they apply only to certain situations or people, effectively 'compartmentalizing' His attributes? How must this passage reshape your understanding of His complete and undivided nature in every aspect of your life?
?
The a fortiori argument (if connected composition is impossible, separate composition is even more so) demands logical consistency in your belief about God. Where in your spiritual walk have you been inconsistent—professing tawhid yet living as if God is limited by your circumstances, your success, or your failures? What concrete step will you take this week to align your actions with the reality of His absolute simplicity and perfection?

The Impossibility of Two Gods Cooperating
Sanusi proves that cooperation between two gods entails composition and impotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why cooperation between two gods is impossible according to Sanusi, as it leads to incapability, composition, or partitioning of divine attributes.

Core Idea
Cooperation between gods implies each is deficient, contradicting divinity.

Sanusi presents a crisp argument against the idea that two gods could cooperate. Cooperation, by its nature, means that neither can achieve the goal alone, which would make each less than fully capable. But incapability negates divinity. Then he goes further: if you try to salvage the idea by saying the combined entity is god, you run into absurdities of composition and partitioning. This passage sharpens the Islamic understanding of tawḥīd by eliminating any possibility of shared divinity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the short but dense structure.
2
Identify the three arguments
Sanusi gives three reasons why cooperation is impossible. Find them: (1) cooperation entails individual incapability, (2) the combined entity would be composed of separate essences, (3) each would have only part of power and will.
These reasons build on each other.
3
Understand the logic of incapability
Reflect on why cooperation implies each god alone is incapable. If two gods can only produce an effect together, then neither can do it alone—that is a deficiency.
Incapability (ʿajz) negates divinity.
4
Contemplate the composition argument
If the combined entity is considered the god, then the god is made of two separate essences. But composition of the divine from parts is impossible.
Sanusi compares it to composition from conjoined essences, which is also impossible.
5
Apply the lesson
Write a short summary in your own words explaining why two gods cannot cooperate, using Sanusi's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمجموعهما أثر ، ثم مآلُ هذا الاجتماع المفروض أنه إثباتُ إلهين كلُّ واحدٍ منهما عاجز ! والعجز ينافي الألوهيَّةَ على ما سبق في صفات الإله • فإن زعم المقدِّرُ أنَّ مجموعهما هو الإله ، لا كلَّ واحدٍ منهما . . كان ذلك تركيباً للإلهِ مِنْ ذاتين منفصلتين ، وكلُّ هذا ممَّا لا يُعقَلُ لعاقل ، وإذا استحال تركيب الإلهِ مِنْ ذاتين متصلتين فكيف يتركَّبُ مِنْ ذاتين منفصلتين ؟! ويؤدي ذلك أيضاً إلى أن يكونَ القائم بكلِّ واحدٍ منهما بعضَ القدرةِ وبعضَ الإرادةِ ، وذلك تجزئة للمعنى ! وهو ظاهر الاستحالة .
There is an effect for both of them combined. Then the outcome of this supposed cooperation is the affirmation of two gods, each of which is incapable! And incapability negates divinity according to what preceded in the attributes of God. • If the one who supposes claims that their combined entity is the god, not each one individually... that would be a composition of the god from two separate essences, and all of this is inconceivable to any rational person. If composition of the god from two conjoined essences is impossible, how could it be composed from two separate essences?! And that also leads to each one of them possessing part of power and part of will, and that is a partitioning of the attribute! And it is clearly impossible.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the possibility of two gods cooperating. Sanusi argues that if two gods were to cooperate on an action, then each individually would be incapable of performing that action alone, which contradicts divinity because incapability (عَجْز) negates godhood. Furthermore, if one claims that the combined entity is the god, then the god would be composed of two separate essences (ذاتين منفصلتين), which is inconceivable. If composition from conjoined essences is impossible, how can it be from separate ones? Moreover, each would possess only part of power and will, which is a partitioning (تجزئة) of the divine attribute, clearly impossible.

Key Terms
عجز (incapability)
تركيب (composition)
ذاتين منفصلتين (two separate essences)
تجزئة (partitioning)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think two gods could cooperate without each being deficient, like two humans working together.
Correction: Sanusi shows that cooperation logically entails that neither can accomplish the task alone, which is a form of deficiency (عجز). Since God must be free from all deficiency, cooperation is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi argue that cooperation between two gods is impossible?
A) Because it would make each god equal in power
B) Because it would necessitate each god being incapable alone, leading to composition
C) Because cooperation implies they are not one
D) Because they would have to share will

Key Takeaway
Two gods cannot cooperate because cooperation implies each is incapable individually, leading to composition or partitioning of divine attributes — all impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if God alone is completely sufficient, or do you find yourself relying on created means as if they have independent power? How does Sanusi's argument challenge that subtle reliance?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you ever think that God needs 'help' from others to fulfill His will? Examine your heart: does this lesson's proof that cooperation implies deficiency expose any hidden shirk in your trust?
?
If the very concept of two gods is rationally impossible, what does that mean for your own tendency to ascribe power or control to anything other than Allah? Will you commit to recognizing His absolute oneness in every moment today?

The Exclusive Power of Allah in Creation
Sanusi asserts that all actions are from Allah alone, who acts by His will without need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all actions and created things are exclusively from Allah, who acts by His will without need or renewal, and that attributing effect to other than Allah is a grave error.

Core Idea
All actions belong to Allah alone, who does as He wills without need.

Sanusi here establishes a core tawhidic principle: every action and created thing has no doer except Allah. He explains that Allah may choose to act in certain ways and not others, purely according to His will, without any purpose or renewal in His attributes. This teaching directly confronts those who ascribe causality to created beings, leading them astray.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic and English to get the full meaning.
Notice the tone of absolute authority.
2
Identify the key claims
Identify the three claims: 1) no doer but Allah, 2) Allah acts as He wills without need, 3) those who ascribe effect to others are misguided.
3
Reflect on the error refuted
Understand that Sanusi is refuting those who think creatures have independent power. This is a crucial point in Islamic theology.
The passage ends with a remark about two supposed gods agreeing on an action, which ties back to the refutation of dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنَّما الرفعُ وغيرُهُ مِنْ جميع الكائناتِ لا فاعل له إلا الإله جلَّ وتعالى ، وقد يختارُ أنْ يفعل فعلاً معَ شيء ، ولا يختارُ فعلَهُ معَ شيء آخرَ ، ويختارُ فعلَهُ في زمان دونَ زمان ، أو في حالة دون حالة ، على حسَبِ ما جرَتْ بهِ مشيئته وحكمُهُ النافذ بلا غرض ولا تجدد لإرادتِهِ وقدرته ، ولا عجز في قدرتِهِ ألبتة ، وإنَّما هو مَلِكُ يفعل ما يشاءُ ويختارُ ، لا يُسأل عما يفعلُ تبارك وتعالى ، وهذا الاختلافُ الذي جرى على وَفْقِ المشيئةِ هو الذي ضلَّ  ِبهِ مَنْ لا بصيرةَ له ولا دينَ ، حتى أسند التأثير لما سواه جل وعزَّ واحد هذا كله ما يتعلَّق بتقديرِ اتفاقِ الإلهين المفروضين على فعل
And indeed lifting and other than it from all created things — no doer of it except Allah, mighty and majestic. He may choose to do an action with something and not choose to do it with something else, and He chooses to do it at one time and not another, or in one state and not another, according to what His will and His penetrating decree necessitate, without purpose or renewal of His will and power, and there is no incapability in His power at all. Rather, He is a King who does what He wills and chooses. He is not asked about what He does, blessed and exalted. And this difference that occurs in accordance with the will is that by which those who have no insight or religion go astray, until they attribute effect to other than Him, mighty and majestic. One. All this is related to the assumption of the two supposed gods agreeing on an action.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement on divine power. Sanusi clarifies that all actions (like raising) and all created things have no doer except Allah alone. Allah has absolute freedom to choose when and with what to act, without any purpose or renewal in His attributes. He is not asked about what He does. The difference in how He acts is due to His will, and those who ascribe effect to other than Allah are misguided. The passage ends with a remark that all this concerns the assumption of two gods agreeing on an action.

Key Terms
فاعل (doer)
مشيئة (will)
حكمه النافذ (penetrating decree)
غرض (purpose)
تجدد (renewal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's different acts imply change in Him.
Correction: Sanusi denies any renewal (تجدد) in Allah's will or power, so His differing acts are without change.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the true state of all actions and creatures?
A) They are produced by Allah alone without any partner
B) They are produced by both Allah and human power
C) They are produced by natural causes independent of Allah
D) They are produced by the two gods

Key Takeaway
All actions and created things are exclusively from Allah, who acts according to His will without need or renewal, and ascribing effect to others is a grave error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something good, do you immediately feel a sense of personal accomplishment? How does Sanusi's declaration that no action has a doer except Allah challenge that feeling? What would it mean for you to truly internalize that your lifting of a hand is from Allah alone?
?
Consider a time you were frustrated because something didn't happen the way you wanted. How does the teaching that Allah chooses to act at one time and not another, according to His will without purpose, reshape your understanding of that disappointment? Are you willing to accept that His differential action is not arbitrary but based on His decree, even when you don't understand?
?
The passage says Allah is not asked about what He does. How comfortable are you with that reality in your own life? Are there areas where you find yourself questioning or demanding an explanation from Allah? What would it take for you to rest in His sovereignty?

The Impossibility of Two Conflicting Wills
Sanusi proves that if two gods conflict, both must be incapable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of two gods with conflicting wills leads to both being incapable, thereby proving the impossibility of multiple gods.

Core Idea
Two opposing gods cannot both be powerful; one must fail, and the winner is also weak.

Sanusi presents a logical proof against the possibility of two gods whose wills conflict. He argues that if two gods disagree on a single event, either both wills are ineffective or one prevails. Both scenarios lead to incapability for both, contradicting the nature of divinity. This passage is a foundational argument for tawheed.

Guided Path
1
Set up the scenario
Read the passage's initial assumption: two gods with conflicting wills on the same thing, like motion vs rest.
2
Follow the logical options
Notice Sanusi considers three possibilities: both wills effective (impossible), both ineffective, or one effective.
Focus on the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the 'similarity' argument
The key point is that even the god whose will prevails is shown to be incapable because he could not will the opposite.
Read the marginal note for clarification.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Reflect on how this argument supports the Islamic doctrine that Allah is one and unique, with no partner in divinity.
This is a core tawheed proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا إِنْ قُدرَ اختلافهما - كأن يريد أحدهما حركة جسم في زمان مثلاً ، ويريد الآخرُ سكونَهُ في ذلكَ الزمن - : فلا خفاءَ أَنَّ العجز هاهنا ظاهرُ اللزوم ؛ لأنَّ نفوذ إرادتهما معاً في هذا الفَرْضِ لا يمكنُ ؛ لما فيهِ مِنَ الجمعِ بين الضدَّينِ ، فلم يبقَ إلا عدم نفوذ إرادتهما معاً ، فيلزم عجزُهما معاً ، ويلزم أيضاً عُرُةُ الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون ، أو نفوذ إرادة أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ ، وذلك أيضاً يستلزم عجزهما معاً ؛ أمَّا الذي لم تنفذ إرادته فظاهرٌ ، وأمَّا الذي فُرضَ نفوذُ إرادتِهِ فلانَّهُ مماثل للذي وَجَبَ عجزه (۱) ، فيجب عجزُهُ هو أيضاً ضرورةً . (۱) في هامش ( هـ ) : ( أي : حيث لم يقدر على ما هو ممكن في نفسه ؛ وهو إرادة الضد ، ويلزم منه عجز القادر ؛ لما مرَّ من انعقاد المماثلة بينهما ) .
As for if their disagreement is supposed — such that one wills the movement of a body at a time, for example, and the other wills its rest at that time — then it is not hidden that incapability is necessarily apparent here. Because the effectiveness of both wills together in this assumption is not possible, due to what it involves of combining two opposites. So nothing remains except the ineffectiveness of both wills together, thus both are necessarily incapable. And also necessary is the body being devoid of motion and rest, or the effectiveness of one will without the other, and that also entails both being incapable. As for the one whose will is not effective, it is apparent. And as for the one whose will is supposed to be effective, because he is similar to the one whose incapability is necessarily established, his incapability is also necessarily established. (1) In the margin (H): (i.e., because he was not able to do what is possible in itself, which is willing the opposite, and that entails the incapability of the capable one, due to what has passed of the establishment of similarity between them).
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a proof against two gods with conflicting wills. If one wills motion and the other wills rest at the same time, then either both wills are ineffective (impossible because then both gods are impotent), or one will succeeds and the other fails. If one fails, that one is obviously impotent. The one who succeeds is similar to the one who failed because he could not will the opposite, so he is also impotent. The marginal note explains: the one who succeeded could not will the opposite, which is possible in itself, so he is incapable. Thus, both are necessarily incapable, which contradicts divinity.

Key Terms
اختلاف (disagreement)
ضدين (two opposites)
نفوذ إرادة (effectiveness of will)
عجز (incapability)
مماثلة (similarity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if one god's will prevails, it proves his power.
Correction: Sanusi shows that inability to will the opposite implies impotence, so the prevailing god is also incapable.

Checkpoint
Why does Sanusi conclude that both gods are incapable when they have conflicting wills?
A) Because neither will can be effective against the other
B) Because the god whose will succeeds could not will the opposite, showing impotence
C) Because both wills cancel each other out
D) Because they both have equal power

Key Takeaway
Two conflicting wills in a single universe necessarily imply inability in both beings, proving that divinity must be singular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that Allah alone has absolute power and will, then why do you sometimes act as if the approval of people or your own desires competes with His command? What would it mean for your daily choices if you lived as though no other will could ever prevail against His?
?
The proof shows that any being who cannot will the opposite of what he wills is weak. When you fail to obey Allah in a certain matter, do you realize that you are acting as if your own will is independent of His? How can you train your heart to submit fully, knowing that any reservation implies a kind of shirk in will?
?
Sanusi argues that if two gods had conflicting wills, both would be incapable. How does this truth expose the futility of ever prioritizing your own desires over Allah's? Are there areas in your life where you are still trying to maintain a 'dual sovereignty' — serving Allah and your own whims? What will you do to break that today?

Objection: Dividing the World Between Two Gods
Sanusi presents an objection about two gods dividing dominion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a common objection to the argument for the oneness of Allah: that two gods could simply divide dominion to avoid conflict.

Core Idea
Can two gods avoid conflict by dividing the world?

The theologian anticipates a possible objection: if two gods exist, why couldn't they simply divide the world into separate domains? This would seem to avoid the problems of cooperation and conflict. The objection is presented here, and the answer follows in the next lesson. This passage helps you think through the logic of divine unity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it begins with 'If you say' (فإن قلت), which signals an anticipated objection.
The objector is proposing a hypothetical scenario.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the words 'division' (قسم) and 'administration' (تدبير). The objector suggests a division of administrative control.
These terms are central to the objection.
3
Consider the underlying assumption
Ask yourself: what must be true for this objection to work? The objector assumes that two gods could each be limited to a part of the world without affecting their divinity.
Think about what attributes a god must have.
4
Reflect on the challenge
This objection challenges the proof of divine unity. If it stands, the proof fails. Consider how you might answer it before seeing the actual answer.
This will prepare you for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : بِمَ يُرَدُّ على تقدير أنَّ الإلهين اختارا قَسْمَ العالم بينهما في التدبير ؛ بحيثُ ينفرد كلُّ واحدٍ منهما بتدبير ما في يده ،
If you say: How is it refuted, on the assumption that the two gods chose to divide the world between them in administration, such that each one of them is solely responsible for administering what is in his hand?
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is an objection (فإن قلت) presented by a hypothetical questioner. He suggests that the two gods might divide the world between themselves, each taking responsibility for a part. This would avoid the problems of cooperation and conflict. The answer is not given in the available text. The lesson presents the objection only.

Key Terms
قسم
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: One might think that dividing the world solves the problem of two gods conflicting, making a two-god scenario plausible.
Correction: The objection itself is not accepted; it is answered in subsequent text (not in this passage). The answer likely shows that such division still implies each god is limited, contradicting the definition of divinity.

Checkpoint

A) That two gods could each will the same thing
B) That two gods could divide the world and each rule a part

D) That two gods cannot exist at all

Key Takeaway
The objection that two gods could simply divide the world is an important challenge that tests the logic of divine unity, requiring careful response.

The Proof of al-Tamānu' for God's Uniqueness in Actions
Sanusi presents the full proof of al-tamānu' for God's exclusive creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof of al-tamānu' (mutual hinderance) and how it demonstrates that God alone is the exclusive creator of all events.

Core Idea
No two independent causes can produce one effect; only God's power is truly effective.

Sanusi now deploys a powerful rational argument known as the proof of mutual hinderance (al-tamānu') to demonstrate that God's uniqueness extends not only to His essence but also to His actions. This proof shows that if any being other than God had genuine causal influence, contradictions would arise. In this lesson, you will trace the logical steps of this proof and see why God must be the sole creator.

Guided Path
1
Understand the aim of the proof
Sanusi states that the same proof used to negate a second god also proves God's sole creative power. This proof targets the idea that anything else can have independent causal influence.
The aim is to show that no creature can be a true cause alongside God.
2
First scenario: Two powers on the same effect
Assume a created being has influence over an effect that is also within God's power. Then the effect's occurrence leads to three impossible consequences: from both (one effect, two independent causes), from one (preponderance without a preponderator), or from neither (the event creates itself).
The impossibility of one effect from two independent causes is key.
3
Second scenario: Opposite effects
Assume the two powers aim for incompatible outcomes (e.g., motion vs. stillness). Then three possibilities arise: both occur (impossible due to contradiction), neither occurs (impossible: body devoid of both and God is impotent), or one occurs (if the other's occurs, God is disabled; if God's occurs, again preponderance without a preponderator).
This scenario covers cases where the powers are directed to mutually exclusive objects.
4
Draw the conclusion
Both scenarios lead to absurdities if a second cause is admitted. Therefore, the attribute of influence and creation is exclusive to God alone.
The proof solidifies tawhid in actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : أنَّ دليل التمانع الذي يدلُّ على استحالة وجودِ إلـه ثانٍ معَ مولانا جل وعزَّ هو بعينه يدلُّ على وجوبِ وحدانيته تعالى في أفعاله ؛ بمعنى : أَنَّهُ يجب انفرادهُ تعالى باختراع جميع الحوادث بلا واسطةٍ ولا أثر لكلّ ما سواه في أثر ما على العموم . وتقرير دليل التمانعِ على هذا المطلبِ : أن نقول : لو صح أن يكون لشيء غيرِ مولانا جل وعزَّ تأثير في أثرٍ ما .. لكانَ ذلك الأثر يجبُ أنْ يكونَ مقدوراً لمولانا جل وعزَّ ومراداً لهُ ؛ لما عرفْتَ مِنْ وجوب عمومِ التعلَّقِ لإرادتِهِ تعالى وقدرته ، فإذا لزم ذلك فوقوع هذا الأثر لا يخلو : إما أن يكون لهما معاً : وهو محال ؛ لاستحالة وقوع أثر واحدٍ بمؤثرين مستقلين ؛ إذِ الفَرْضُ استقلال كلُّ واحدٍ منهما باختراع هذا الأثر ، وإما أن يكونَ بأحدهما : فيلزم الترجيح بلا مُرجح ، وأيضاً يلزمُ من تخلف هذا الأثر عن أحدهما جواز تخلفه عن الآخرِ ؛ إِذِ الفَرْضُ استواؤهما بالنسبة إلى هذا الأثر ، وذلك مستلزم لجواز تعجيز قدرة المولى جل وعز . وإنْ فُرِضَ ذلكَ الأثرُ لم يقع بواحدٍ منهما : لزم وقوع الحادثِ بنفسِهِ ، ولزم تعجيزُ القدرة القديمة ، وكلاهما محال . هذا كله إنْ قُدْرَ توارد قدرة الإلهِ تعالى وقدرة شيء مما سواه على مقدور واحد . وأَمَّا إِنْ قُدِّرَ اختلافُهما - كأن نفرض تعلُّق قدرةِ المولى جل وعزَّ وإرادته بحركة جسم في زمان كذا ، وتعلق قدرة الغير بسكون ذلكَ الجسمِ في ذلك الزمانِ المعيَّنِ - فحينئذٍ نقولُ : لا يخلو : إما أن يقع الأمران جميعاً ، فيلزم عليه اجتماع الضدين وهما الحركة والسكون ، أو لا يقعَ واحد منهما ، فيلزم عجز الباري تعالى عن ذلكَ ، ويلزم أيضاً عُرُو ذلكَ الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون ، أو يقع أحدهما دونَ الآخرِ ، فإنْ كانَ ذلكَ الواقع هو مقدور الغيرِ لزم تعجيزُ الإله جلَّ وعزَّ ، وإنْ كانَ الواقع مقدور المولى جل وعزَّ لزم الترجيحُ بلا مُرجُحٍ ، ولزم أيضاً جواز عجزِ القدرة القديمة ؛ لفَرْضِ مساواتِها لقدرة الغير بالنسبة لهذا الأثر . فبانَ لكَ بهذا : أنَّ صفة التأثيرِ والاختراع للكائناتِ مِنْ خواص مولانا جل وعزَّ التي لا يشاركه فيها شيءٌ مِنْ جميع ما سواه .
He means: The proof of mutual hinderance which proves the impossibility of the existence of a second god with our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, is the very same thing that proves the obligation of His uniqueness, exalted be He, in His actions; in the sense that it is necessary for Him, exalted be He, to be alone in inventing all events without any intermediary and without any effect for anything other than Him in any effect at all, universally. The demonstration of the proof of mutual hinderance for this demand is: We say: If it were valid that something other than our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, has influence in some effect, then that effect must be within the power of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, and willed by Him; because you have known the obligation of the universality of attachment for His will and power, exalted be He. If that is necessary, then the occurrence of this effect is not free: either it is from both together — and that is impossible due to the impossibility of the occurrence of one effect by two independent causes, for the assumption is the independence of each one of them in inventing this effect; or it is from one of them — then preponderance without a preponderator becomes necessary, and also the non-occurrence of this effect from one of them necessitates the permissibility of its non-occurrence from the other, since the assumption is their equality with respect to this effect, and that entails the permissibility of disabling the power of the Master, may He be glorified and exalted; and if that effect is assumed not to occur by either of them, then the occurrence of the event by itself would be necessary, and the disabling of the eternal power would be necessary, and both are impossible. All this is under the assumption of the concurrence of the power of God, exalted be He, and the power of something else upon one possible object. As for the case where they differ — such as if we assume the attachment of the power of the Master, may He be glorified and exalted, and His will to the motion of a body at a certain time, and the attachment of the power of another to the stillness of that body at that specific time — then we say: It is not free: either both matters occur, in which case the conjunction of two opposites, namely motion and stillness, is necessary; or neither occurs, in which case the incapacity of the Creator, exalted be He, for that becomes necessary, and also the divesting of that body from motion and stillness becomes necessary; or one of them occurs without the other: if that which occurs is the object of the power of the other, then the disabling of God, may He be glorified and exalted, becomes necessary; and if that which occurs is the object of the power of the Master, may He be glorified and exalted, then preponderance without a preponderator becomes necessary, and also the permissibility of the incapacity of the eternal power becomes necessary, due to the assumption of its equality with the power of the other with respect to this effect. So it has become clear to you by this: The attribute of influence and invention of existents is among the specific properties of our Master, may He be glorified and exalted, in which nothing from all other than Him shares.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a formal proof (دليل التمانع) for God's uniqueness in actions (وحدانية الأفعال). The author begins by stating that the same proof used to show the impossibility of a second god also proves that God must be alone in creating all events. The proof is then presented in two main scenarios. First scenario: if a created being had influence on an effect, that effect must also be within God's power and will due to the universality of God's attributes. Then the effect either occurs from both (impossible because one effect cannot have two independent causes), from one (leading to preponderance without a preponderator and implying God's power could be disabled), or from neither (impossible because the event would occur by itself and God's power would be disabled). This first scenario assumes the two powers are directed to the same effect. Second scenario: the two powers are directed to opposite effects (e.g., motion vs. stillness). Then either both occur (impossible because opposites cannot coexist), neither occurs (implying God's incapacity and the body being devoid of both), or one occurs. If the other's occurs, God is disabled; if God's occurs, again preponderance without a preponderator and possible incapacity arise. The conclusion is clear: the attribute of influence and creation is exclusive to God, with no partner.

Key Terms
دليل التمانع
وحدانية الأفعال
استقلال
ترجيح بلا مرجح
توارد القدرة
اجتماع الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of al-tamānu' only applies to proving that there is no second god, not to God's actions.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the very same proof demonstrates God's uniqueness in His actions — that He alone creates all events without partner.

Checkpoint
In the first scenario of the proof of al-tamānu', what is the consequence if the effect occurs from only one of the two supposed causes?
A) The effect must occur by itself.
B) It necessitates preponderance without a preponderator and implies possible incapacity of God's power.
C) Both causes are equally effective.
D) The effect is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The proof of al-tamānu' shows that any supposed independent cause besides God leads to logical contradictions, affirming that only God is the true creator of all that exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing in your life happens except by God's direct creation, or do you still attribute your successes to your own efforts? What would change if you lived with the full conviction that every breath is a direct act of God?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you instinctively fear the created means (like illness, people, or circumstances) as independent causes, or do you recognize that only God's power is effective? How can you train your heart to see only His hand in everything?
?
If you truly internalize that God alone invents all events, how would it transform your supplication (du'a) and your reliance (tawakkul)? Are you willing to let go of false dependencies on other than Him?
