B) Emission of rays from the eye
C) A substrate (maḥall) in which it subsists
D) Absence of any veil

Key Takeaway
Seeing Allah is a non-physical perception that requires neither organs, direction, facing, rays, nor any physical condition—only a soul capable of receiving that vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah in a physical form or direction, even unintentionally? How can you train your soul to perceive Him without those creaturely limitations, starting today?
?
The text says that nothing physical—close or far, thick veil or ray—prevents seeing Allah, just as nothing prevents knowledge. Does your daily life reflect this belief, or do you act as if your circumstances (busyness, distractions) block your knowledge of Him? What concrete step can you take to remove that feeling of distance?
?
If seeing Allah requires no eye, no direction, no facing—only a soul ready to perceive—what are you doing right now to prepare that soul? What attachment or distraction is the thickest veil you need to lift?

Perceiving God by Other Senses: The Safer Position
Sanusi discusses the dispute over perceiving God by smell, taste, and touch, and recommends refraining.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dispute about perceiving God through smell, taste, and touch, and why Sanusi recommends refraining from affirming or denying such perceptions.

Core Idea
The safest approach to divine perception is to stop where revelation stops.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a subtle theological question: can God be perceived by senses other than sight, specifically smell, taste, and touch? He clarifies the unanimous impossibility of physical contact with God, then explores the contested possibility of non-physical perception through these senses. By using an everyday example, he shows that the act of sensing does not necessarily entail perception. Finally, he advises caution: without proof, it is better to refrain from both affirming and denying such perceptions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the consensus on physical sensing
Sanusi first states there is no dispute that God cannot be physically smelled, tasted, or touched. Note why: those senses require bodies and accidents.
2
Identify the real point of disagreement
The dispute is about perceiving God by these senses without any physical contact. This is a more abstract question.
3
Analyze the apple analogy
Sanusi gives an example: you can smell an apple without perceiving its smell. This shows that the act of sensing and the perception are separable.
The analogy supports the possibility that perceptions can occur without bodies.
4
Evaluate the evidence and the recommended position
No proof has been established for actual occurrence. Therefore, Sanusi says it is safer to refrain from affirming or denying such perceptions.
This is a practical theological caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نزاع في امتناع كونه تعالى مشموماً مذوقاً ملموساً ؛ لاختصاص ذلك بالأجسام والأعراض ، وإنَّما النزاع في إدراكه بالشم والذوق واللمس مِنْ غير اتصال بالحواس . وحاصله : كما أنَّ الشم والذوق واللمس لا تستلزم الإدراك ؛ لصحة قولنا : ( شممتُ التفاح وذقتُهُ ولمستُه ، فما أدركتُ رائحته وطعمه وكيفيَّتَهُ ) (۱) . . كذلك أنواع الإدراكات الحاصلة عند الشم والذوقِ واللمس لا تستلزمها ، بل يمكن أن تحصل بدونها ، وتتعلَّقَ بغير الأجسام والأعراض . وأما الوقوع : لم يقم عليهِ دليلٌ ، والأولى الاكتفاء بالرؤيةِ ، والوقف عن هذه الإدراكاتِ جوازاً ووقوعاً ، فهو أسلم وأحوط (۲) .
There is no dispute about the impossibility of Him being smelled, tasted, or touched, because that is specific to bodies and accidents. Rather, the dispute is about perceiving Him by smell, taste, and touch without connection to the senses. Its summary: Just as smell, taste, and touch do not entail perception, due to the correctness of our saying: 'I smelled the apple, tasted it, and touched it, but I did not perceive its smell, taste, or quality,' likewise the types of perceptions that occur during smell, taste, and touch are not entailed by them; rather they can occur without them and relate to other than bodies and accidents. As for occurrence: no proof has been established for it, and it is better to suffice with vision and to refrain from these perceptions in terms of possibility and occurrence, for it is safer and more cautious.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage clarifies the disagreement about perceiving God by senses other than sight. It first notes that there is no dispute that God cannot be physically smelled, tasted, or touched, as those are properties of bodies and accidents. The real dispute is about the perception itself (the act of smelling, tasting, or touching) without physical contact. The passage draws an analogy: our everyday experience shows that the physical actions of smelling, tasting, and touching do not necessarily result in perception (e.g., one can smell an apple without actually perceiving its scent). Similarly, the perceptions that occur during these actions are not necessarily tied to bodies and accidents; they could occur without them. However, as for actual occurrence of such perception of God, there is no evidence. Therefore, the safest and most cautious position is to suffice with the vision (ru'ya) and to refrain from affirming or denying these perceptions, both in terms of possibility and occurrence.

ذوق

جواز
وقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that physical sensing implies perception of a body, so if God is not a body, He cannot be perceived by senses at all.
Correction: The analogy shows that acts of sensing and perceptions are separable. Perception can occur without a physical act of sensing, and can relate to non-bodily realities. So the possibility is not automatically ruled out.

Checkpoint
What is the safest position regarding perceiving God by senses other than sight?
A) It is forbidden to claim it is possible.
B) It is obligatory to believe it occurs.
C) It is better to refrain from both affirming possibility and occurrence.
D) It is permissible based on analogy with sight.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi advises theological restraint: without proof, it is safest to refrain from affirming or denying that God can be perceived by smell, taste, or touch.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you are certain of God's existence but uncertain about a detail, do you have the humility to stop, or do you feel compelled to speculate? Reflect on a specific question about God you have been entertaining without proof.
?
Sanusi teaches that it is safer to refrain. What is one belief about God that you hold without clear evidence from revelation or reason? Will you commit to letting it go today?

On Perceiving God by Smell, Taste, and Touch
Sanusi argues against the necessity and actuality of perceiving God by smell, taste, and touch.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ashʿarī tradition considers it safer to abstain from affirming or denying that God can be perceived by smell, taste, or touch, and why vision (ruʾya) is sufficient.

Core Idea
Perceiving God by the lower senses is neither necessary nor proven; vision alone is the safe path.

Sanusi tackles a subtle question: can we perceive God through senses other than sight? While it is agreed that God cannot be touched, smelled, or tasted physically, the possibility of a non‑physical perception remains debated. Sanusi argues that such perceptions are not entailed by the sensory acts and that no proof confirms their occurrence. He recommends sticking to the established doctrine of vision (ruʾya) as the safer, more precautionary position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the uncontroversial premise
Recognize that no one disputes the impossibility of God being physically smelled, tasted, or touched—these are attributes of bodies and accidents.
This sets the foundation: the debate is not about physical contact.
2
Grasp the distinction between act and perception
Sanusi uses the analogy of an apple: you can perform the action of smelling, tasting, or touching without actually perceiving the object's qualities. The sensory act does not entail the perception.
This shows that perceptions can occur independently of the physical senses.
3
Follow the conclusion to abstain and rely on vision
Since no proof exists for the actual occurrence of such perceptions, and to be safe, we should suffice with the vision of God (al-ruʾya) and refrain from affirming or denying the possibility or occurrence of these other perceptions.
This is the recommended cautious approach in Ashʿarī theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا نزاع في امتناع كونه تعالى مشموماً مذوقاً ملموساً ; لاختصاص ذلك بالأجسام والأعراض , وإنما النزاع في إدراكه بالشم والذوق واللمس من غير اتصال بالحواس وحاصله : كما أن الشم والذوق واللمس لا تستلزم الإدراك ; لصحة قولنا : ( شممت التفاح وذقته ولمسته , فما أدركت رائحته وطعمه وكيفيته ) . . كذلك أنواع الإدراكات الحاصلة عند الشم والذوق واللمس لا تستلزمها , بل يمكن أن تحصل بدونها , وتتعلق بغير الأجسام والأعراض وأما الوقوع : لم يقم عليه دليل , والأولى الاكتفاء بالرؤية , والوقف عن هذه الإدراكات جوازاً ووقوعاً , فهو أسلم وأحوط , وبالله التوفيق (1) (2)
There is no dispute concerning the impossibility of His being smelled, tasted, touched — because that is specific to bodies and accidents. Rather, the dispute concerns perceiving Him by smell, taste, and touch without connection to the senses. Its summary: just as smell, taste, and touch do not entail perception — for the correctness of our saying: 'I smelled the apple, tasted it, and touched it, but I did not perceive its smell, taste, and quality' — likewise the types of perceptions occurring at smell, taste, and touch do not entail them, but rather can occur without them, and relate to other than bodies and accidents. As for actual occurrence, no proof has been established for it, and the better course is to suffice with vision (al-ruʾya) and to abstain from these perceptions in terms of permissibility and occurrence, for it is safer and more precautionary. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the Ashʿarī position on whether God can be perceived by the lower senses (smell, taste, touch). Sanusi begins by stating an uncontroversial premise: it is impossible for God to be smelled, tasted, or touched in the ordinary sense, because those qualities belong to bodies and accidents. The real dispute is whether God can be perceived by these senses without physical contact. He then clarifies that the act of smelling, tasting, or touching does not necessarily produce a perception — one can perform the physical action without actually perceiving the object's smell, taste, or quality. Similarly, the perceptions that sometimes accompany these actions are not necessitated by them; they could occur independently and relate to non‑physical realities. As for whether such perceptions have ever actually occurred, no evidence supports it. The safest position is to rely on the vision of God (al-ruʾya) and to refrain from affirming or denying the possibility or actuality of these other perceptions. This cautious approach is both more sound and more scrupulous. The passage ends with the phrase 'wa billāh al-tawfīq' (and with God is success).

Key Terms
شم
ذوق
لمس
إدراك
رؤية
جواز
وقوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God cannot be perceived by the senses, then He is not real or that denying such perceptions implies denial of God's existence.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the issue is about the modality of perception, not about existence. God is real, but the senses other than sight do not provide proof of His perception.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the safest position regarding perceiving God by smell, taste, or touch?
A) To affirm that it is possible
B) To deny that it is possible
C) To suffice with vision and abstain from these perceptions
D) To insist that it must happen for the believer

Key Takeaway
The safest position regarding perceiving God by lower senses is to restrict ourselves to the vision of God and refrain from affirming or denying such perceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that there is no proof for perceiving God by smell, taste, or touch, and the safest course is to abstain. Have you ever attributed to God a quality or experience based on your own imagination or wishful thinking, rather than on what has been revealed? How will you guard your heart from such assumptions starting today?
?
The passage uses an apple analogy to show that sensory acts do not guarantee perception. In your spiritual life, are you merely 'going through the motions' of worship (like smelling without perceiving) without genuinely perceiving God's presence? What specific step will you take to move from the act to genuine perception?
?
Sanusi advises to suffice with vision and abstain from affirming or denying other perceptions. Where in your life do you need to exercise intellectual humility and refrain from making assertions about matters where revelation is silent? What is one area where you have been too confident in your own opinions about God?

The Objection of Companions' Hesitation and Its Answer
Sanusi presents and answers the objection about Companions' hesitation during compilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the Companions' verification of Qur'anic surahs undermines the doctrine of i'jaz al-nazm (miraculous composition).

Core Idea
Caution, not confusion: the Companions’ verification protected against even trivial errors.

One might wonder: if the Qur'an's composition is miraculously eloquent, why did the Companions require testimony to confirm surahs like al-Fatiha and al-Mu'awwidhatayn? This objection, attributed to Ibn Mas'ud, seems to challenge the self-evident nature of the miracle. Sanusi answers by explaining the Companions' extreme caution and the subtlety of the miracle, while also discrediting the report as fabricated.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the premise: the Companions hesitated and demanded witness testimony for some verses, which suggests the miracle was not obvious. Note the specific mention of Ibn Mas'ud's reported doubt about al-Fatiha and al-Mu'awwidhatayn.
This objection is the second of several arguments against i'jaz al-nazm.
2
Identify the structure of the answer
Sanusi gives a multi-part answer. First, he questions the authenticity of the report. Second, he notes the compilation occurred after the Prophet's death. Third, he clarifies that the Companions' hesitation was out of caution, not uncertainty about the miracle.
The answer also mentions that the miracle of each sura is not immediately obvious to everyone.
3
Examine the footnote
Read the citation from al-Nawawi and Ibn Hazm that declares the report about Ibn Mas'ud as false and fabricated. This refutes the very basis of the objection.
The footnote is part of the original text and reinforces the answer.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
The Companions' verification was a precaution against accidental errors in writing, not a sign of doubt in the Qur'an's miraculousness. The miracle is profound and not always immediately obvious.
This balances textual preservation with theological doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنَّ الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عنهم أجمعين عندَ جَمْعِ القرآن كانوا يتوقفون في بعض السور والآياتِ إلى شهادة الثقاتِ وابن مسعود رضي الله عنه تردَّدَ في ( الفاتحة ) و( المعوذتين ) ، ولو كانَ نظم القرآنِ مُعْجِزاً بفصاحتِهِ لا بالصَّرْفة.. لكان كافياً في الشهادة ، ولم يتردَّدوا وجوابُهُ ـ بعد صحةِ الرواية بما ذُكِرَ (۲) ، وكون الجمع بعد النبي صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لا في زمانه ، وكون كل سورةٍ مُستقلةً بالإعجاز - : أنَّ ذلك كانَ للاحتياط والاحتراز عن أدنى تغيير لا يُخِلُّ بالإعجاز ، وأنَّ إعجاز كلَّ سورةٍ ليسَ ممَّا يظهرُ لكلِّ أحدٍ ابتداءً (۲) قال الإمام النووي في ( المجموع شرح المهذب ) ( ٣٩٦/٣ ) : ( وما نقل عن ابن مسعود في ( الفاتحة » و « المعوذتين » باطل ليس بصحيح عنه ، عنه ، قال ابن حزم في أول كتابه ( المحلى ) ( ۲۱ ) : هذا كذب على ابن مسعود موضوع ، وإنما عنه قراءة عاصم عن زر عن ابن مسعود ، وفيها صح الفاتحة » و « المعوذتان » ) .
The second (argument): that the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them all) at the time of compiling the Qur'an used to pause regarding some surahs and verses until the testimony of trustworthy people, and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him) hesitated regarding (al-Fatiha) and (al-Mu'awwidhatayn), and if the composition of the Qur'an was miraculous by its eloquence, not by sarfah, it would have been sufficient for testimony, and they would not have hesitated. And its answer — after the authenticity of the report concerning what was mentioned (2), and that the compilation was after the Prophet (peace be upon him) not in his time, and that each surah is independently miraculous — is that that was for caution and protection against the slightest alteration that does not impair the miracle, and that the miracle of each surah is not something that appears to everyone initially. (2) Imam al-Nawawi said in (al-Majmu' Sharh al-Muhadhdhab, 3/396): 'What was transmitted from Ibn Mas'ud regarding (al-Fatiha) and (al-Mu'awwidhatayn) is false, not authentic from him, from him.' Ibn Hazm said at the beginning of his book (al-Muhalla, 21): 'This is a lie against Ibn Mas'ud, fabricated. Rather, from him is the qira'a of 'Asim from Zar from Ibn Mas'ud, and in it al-Fatiha and al-Mu'awwidhatayn are correct.'
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection (al-thani) that could be raised against the view that the miracle lies in the composition (nazm) itself. The objection is that the Companions, during the compilation of the Qur'an after the Prophet's death, required testimony from reliable witnesses to confirm certain surahs, and Ibn Mas'ud reportedly hesitated about al-Fatiha and al-Mu'awwidhatayn. If the Qur'an's eloquence alone were sufficient proof of its divine origin, why would they need such verification? The author then gives the answer (jawaabuhu): after confirming the authenticity of that report (with a footnote citing al-Nawawi and Ibn Hazm that the report is false), and noting that the compilation was after the Prophet's time and that each surah is independently miraculous, he explains that their caution was to protect against even the slightest change that would not impair the miracle, and that the miraculous nature of each surah is not immediately apparent to everyone. The footnote further discredits the report about Ibn Mas'ud, showing it is baseless.

Misconception: Some think the Companions' verification indicates uncertainty about the Qur'an's divine origin or that the miracle was unclear even to them.
Correction: The passage clarifies their hesitation was out of extreme caution against textual errors, not doubt about the miracle. Additionally, the report about Ibn Mas'ud is fabricated, so the objection lacks foundation.

Checkpoint

A) The report about Ibn Mas'ud is actually authentic, but the Companions had doubts.
B) Their caution was to guard against even minor alterations that would not affect the miracle.
C) The Companions were not certain of the eloquence.
D) The compilation occurred during the Prophet's lifetime.

Key Takeaway
The Companions' caution in compiling the Qur'an was a protective measure against textual errors, not a sign of doubt in its miraculous composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions went to extraordinary lengths to preserve the Qur'an, verifying even a single letter. How does your own attitude toward the Qur'an's preservation compare to their caution? Do you treat every letter as divine and act accordingly?
?
When you hear a report about Islamic history that seems to cast doubt on the Qur'an's authenticity, do you immediately verify it from authoritative sources, or do you let it unsettle your conviction? What would it take for you to be as rigorous as al-Nawawi and Ibn Hazm in rejecting false reports?
?
Sanusi shows that the miracle of each surah is not immediately obvious to everyone. Where in your life do you assume that because you don't see the miracle, it isn't there? Will you commit to seeking deeper understanding rather than dismissing what you don't yet perceive?

The Prophet's Choice: Servant-Prophet over King-Prophet
Sanusi recounts the Prophet's choice to be a servant-prophet over a king-prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of the Prophet's choice to be a servant-prophet rather than a king-prophet, reflecting his supreme humility and detachment from worldly wealth.

Core Idea
The Prophet chose humility over kingship to embody true devotion.

This lesson presents a remarkable incident where the Prophet Muhammad (PBUH) was offered two options: to be a prophet-king or a prophet-servant. He chose the latter, and even when offered immense wealth, he refused. This story illustrates his profound asceticism and trust in Allah, and it corrects the misconception that his poverty was involuntary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two offers presented to the Prophet: the initial choice of being a servant or a king, and then the offer of gold.
The passage is a single narrative, but contains two distinct events.
2
Identify the two choices
The Prophet was first offered by revelation to be a prophet-servant or a prophet-king. Identify which he chose and why.
The choice of 'servant' indicates humility.
3
Understand the Prophet's response to Gabriel
Gabriel offered to turn mountains into gold for the Prophet, guaranteeing his rank wouldn't diminish. The Prophet refused with a statement about the world. Read his response and reflect on its meaning.
The Prophet's words show his disinterest in worldly gain.
4
Reflect on the spiritual lesson
Consider why the Prophet chose to fast one day and eat one day. The passage says it was to supplicate and give thanks. Think about how this practice fosters humility.
This voluntary hunger is a form of worship.
5
Connect to broader themes
This incident exemplifies zuhd (asceticism) and tawakkul (trust in Allah). Contrast the Prophet's voluntary poverty with the misconception that it was imposed.
The Prophet could have had wealth but chose not to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل قد عُرِضَ عليه بالوحي أن يكون نبياً عبداً أو نبياً مَلِكاً ، فاختار أنْ يكونَ نبيّاً عبداً (۱) ، وعَرَضَ عليهِ جبريلُ أنْ تصيرَ لهُ جبالُ تهامة ذهباً تذهب معه حيثما ذهب ، وضمنَ لهُ معَ ذلكَ ألا ينقص له بسببٍ ذلكَ شيءٌ مِنْ رتبتِهِ التي هي فوق جميعِ الخلائقِ في الآخرة ، فقال : « يَا جِبْرِيلُ ؛ الدُّنْيَا دَارُ مَنْ لا دَارَ لَهُ ، وَيَسْعَى لَهَا مَنْ لا عَقْلَ لَهُ » ، واختار أن يجوع يوماً ويأكل يوماً ؛ ليتضرع ويشكر (۲)
Rather, he was offered by revelation to be a prophet-servant or a prophet-king, and he chose to be a prophet-servant. And Gabriel offered him that the mountains of Tihama become gold for him, going with him wherever he went, and guaranteed him along with that that nothing of his rank (which is above all creatures in the Hereafter) would be diminished because of that. So he said: 'O Gabriel, this world is the abode of one who has no abode, and strives for it one who has no intellect.' And he chose to fast one day and eat one day, so that he might supplicate and give thanks.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage narrates a key event demonstrating the Prophet Muhammad's (PBUH) extraordinary humility and asceticism. It begins by stating that two options were presented to him by revelation: to be a prophet-servant or a prophet-king. He chose the former. Then Gabriel offered him the wealth of the mountains with a guarantee that his rank would not be diminished. The Prophet refused, saying that the world is only for those who have no true home and no intellect. He instead chose periodic fasting to foster supplication and gratitude. The passage highlights that the Prophet's preference for poverty was not due to any lack of ability to have wealth, but out of spiritual excellence.

Correction: The passage shows the Prophet was offered immense wealth (mountains of gold) but he chose poverty out of spiritual excellence, not inability.

A) He was forced by circumstances.
B) He wanted to demonstrate humility and devotion.
C) He feared losing rank if he became a king.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet voluntarily chose a life of humility and poverty to perfect his servitude and gratitude to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Prophet refused gold mountains even when guaranteed no loss of rank. Is there any material comfort, reputation, or worldly gain that you hold so tightly that you would struggle to let go, even for the sake of deeper devotion? What would it take for you to release it?

The Prophet alternated fasting and eating to remain in a state of supplication and gratitude. Do you structure your daily life around comfort and ease, or around spiritual states like neediness and thanks? What one concrete change can you make today to prioritize these attitudes?

Defense of Karamat and Refutation of Deniers
Sanusi criticizes deniers of karamat and presents the correct view

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that karamat (extraordinary events) are permissible for saints (awliya) according to Ahl al-Sunnah, and why denying them stems from ignorance or extremism.

Core Idea
Karamat are real—they flow from purity of creed, not from imagination.

This lesson addresses a common attack against the awliya: the denial of karamat. Sanusi highlights the ignorance behind such denial and even criticizes an extreme view that declares faith in karamat as kufr. Finally, he presents the balanced position of Imam al-Nasafi, affirming that breaking natural customs as a karamah is permissible for the friends of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the emotional tone of the author as he defends the awliya.
2
Identify the two groups criticized
The passage criticizes two groups: the general deniers who attack awliya, and a surprising faqih who called belief in karamat kufr.
Note the strong language used against the deniers.
3
Understand the basis of karamat
Sanusi says the basis is purity of creed, sincerity, following the way, and selecting the truth. Karamat are not random events.
This shows karamat are rooted in spiritual excellence.
4
Examine the extreme position
Be astonished at the faqih’s statement that believing in such extraordinary events is kufr. This is a surprising position from a supposed scholar of Ahl al-Sunnah.
Why would a jurist make such a claim?
5
Learn the correct ruling from al-Nasafi
Imam al-Nasafi rules that breaking custom as a karamah for awliya is permissible according to Ahl al-Sunnah. This is the balanced view.
This settles the matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لم يشاهدوا ذلكَ مِنْ أنفسهم قط ، ولم يسمعوا بهِ مِنْ رؤسائهم الذينَ يزعمون أنهم على شيءٍ ، معَ اجتهادِهم في أمرِ العباداتِ واجتناب السيئات ، فوقعوا في أولياء الله تعالى أصحاب الكراماتِ يُمزقونَ أديمهم ويمضغونَ لحومهم ، ولم يعرفوا أنَّ مبنى هذا الأمر على صفاء العقيدة ، ونقاء السريرةِ ، واقتفاء الطريقةِ ، واصطفاء الحقيقة وإنَّما العجبُ مِنْ بعض فقهاء أهل السنَّةِ ؛ حيثُ قال فيما رُوِيَ عن إبراهيم بن أدهم أنَّهم رأوه بالبصرة يوم التروية ، وفي بمكَّةَ : إِنَّ مَنِ اعتقد جواز ذلكَ يكفرُ ! ذلك اليوم والإنصافُ ما ذكرَهُ الإمامُ النسفي حينَ سُئِلَ عَمَّا يُحكى : : أنَّ الكعبة كانَتْ تزور واحداً مِنَ الأولياء ، هل يجوزُ القولُ بهِ ؟ فقال : نقضُ العادة على سبيل الكرامة لأهل الولاية جائز عند أهل السنة
They have never witnessed that from themselves at all, nor have they heard of it from their leaders who claim to be upon something, despite their diligence in acts of worship and avoiding sins. So they attacked the saints of Allah, the possessors of karamat, tearing their skin and chewing their flesh, and they did not know that the basis of this matter is purity of creed, purity of innermost self, adherence to the way, and selection of the reality. The wonder is from some jurists of Ahl al-Sunnah, who said regarding what was narrated about Ibrahim ibn Adham that they saw him in Basra on the day of Tarwiyah, and in Mecca: 'Indeed, whoever believes in the permissibility of that disbelieves!' That day. And the fair judgment is what Imam al-Nasafi mentioned when asked about what is narrated: that the Kaaba used to visit one of the awliya. Is it permissible to say so? He said: 'Breaking of custom by way of karamah for the people of wilayah is permissible according to Ahl al-Sunnah.'
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of those who deny karamat. The author begins by describing the ignorance of the deniers: they have never experienced karamat themselves nor heard of them from their leaders, yet they attack the awliya. They fail to realize that the foundation of this matter is pure creed and sincere following of the Sharia. The author then expresses astonishment at a certain faqih from Ahl al-Sunnah who, based on a report about Ibrahim ibn Adham being seen in two places on the same day, claimed that believing in such is kufr. This is a harsh position. The author then presents the correct view from Imam al-Nasafi, who ruled that breaking customs as a karamah for awliya is permissible according to Ahl al-Sunnah. The passage thus affirms the legitimacy of karamat and criticizes excessive denial.

Correction: Imam al-Nasafi ruled that such karamat are permissible for awliya and do not diminish prophethood.

Karamat are permissible for awliya, and their denial often arises from lack of experience or extreme juristic positions.

First Objection Against Karamat and Its Refutation
Objection that karamat blur prophethood, refuted by prior distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main objection raised against the possibility of karamat (saintly miracles) and how it is refuted by distinguishing them from prophetic miracles.

Core Idea
Karamat do not confuse prophethood because the context of claim and challenge makes the difference.

This lesson presents one of the key objections to the reality of karamat: that if saints can perform extraordinary acts, how can we tell them apart from prophets? The refutation is brief but decisive, pointing to an earlier distinction already drawn between miracles of prophets (mu'jizat) and saintly wonders (karamat). Understanding this distinction is essential for a proper theology of divine intervention.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage and note the reasoning: karamat would cause confusion with prophethood because miracles are the sign of a prophet.
The objection is that if awliya also perform extraordinary acts, one could not tell prophets apart from them.
2
Identify the refutation
The response is by reference to a prior distinction; what might that distinction be?
3
Reflect on why the refutation works
The distinction lies in the presence of a prophetic claim and a challenge. Without them, the act is a karamah, not a prophecy-miracle.
This preserves the unique sign of prophethood while allowing saintly wonders.
4
Link to broader theology
This shows how careful definitions protect the uniqueness of prophethood while allowing saintly miracles.
5
Check understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm you have grasped the objection and refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج مَنْ أنكر الكراماتِ : بأنَّ خوارق العاداتِ لو ظهرَتْ على يدِ الولي لالتبس النبيُّ بغيره ؛ إذ الفارقُ هو المعجزة . ورُدَّ بِما مَرَّ مِنَ الفَرْقِ بينَهما
And those who deny karamat argued that if extraordinary events appear at the hand of a wali, the prophet would be confused with others, since the distinguishing factor is the miracle. And it was refuted by what has passed regarding the distinction between them.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This is an objection and its answer regarding the possibility of karamat. The deniers argue that if awliya could perform extraordinary acts (khawariq al-‘adat), it would blur the distinction between prophets and non-prophets, because the defining feature of a prophet (mu'jizah) is the same type of phenomenon. The refutation is given by reference to an earlier distinction already established between miracles of prophets (mu'jizat) and karamat of saints. That earlier distinction (not present in this passage) presumably clarifies that a mu'jizah is accompanied by a claim of prophethood and challenge, whereas a karamah is not. Thus the confusion does not arise.

Key Terms
خوارق العادات
ولي
معجزة
فرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any extraordinary event could be a sign of prophethood, so saints performing wonders undermines prophethood.
Correction: The refutation points to a pre-existing distinction: a miracle of prophethood is accompanied by a claim and challenge, whereas a saintly wonder is not. Therefore, confusion does not arise.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the deniers' first objection against karamat?
A) Karamat are not mentioned in Quran
B) If awliya perform miracles, prophets become indistinguishable
C) Karamat contradict reason
D) Awliya cannot have high status

Key Takeaway
The objection that karamat cause confusion with prophethood is refuted by the already-established distinction between prophetic miracles and saintly wonders, based on the context of claim and challenge.

?

Second Objection Against Karamat and Its Refutation
Objection and refutation regarding karamat undermining proof of prophethood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the objection that karamat would undermine proof of prophethood is invalid, and how the refutation hinges on the distinctive role of the prophetic claim.

Core Idea
Karamat do not weaken the proof of prophethood because miracles are tied to a claim.

The second objection against karamat raises a crucial epistemological concern: if saints can perform extraordinary acts, how can we trust miracles as proof of prophethood? The refutation, though brief, is decisive. It draws on the principle that a miracle (mu'jizah) is defined by its accompaniment of a prophetic claim, which karamat lack. Let's unpack this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of objection and refutation.
2
Identify the objection's logic
The objection argues that if karamat can occur without the purpose of attestation (gharad al-tasdiq), then any miracle could be a karamah, closing the door to proving prophethood.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation invokes the earlier definition: a miracle (mu'jizah) is specifically an extraordinary act that accompanies a prophetic claim. In that context, it provides certain attestation (tasdiq qat'i). Thus, karamat, lacking such a claim, do not cause confusion.
This preserves the distinct role of prophethood miracles.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the presence of a claim is crucial. Without it, an extraordinary act could be from a saint or a liar, but with the claim and challenge, it becomes a definitive proof.
This underscores the importance of context in kalam arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو ظهرَتْ لا لغرض التصديقِ لانسدَّ بابُ إثباتِ النبوَّةِ بالمعجزة ؛ لجواز أنْ يكونَ لغيرِ غرض التصديقِ . ورُدَّ بما مرَّ مِنْ أنَّها عند مقارنتها للدعوى تفيد التصديق قطعاً
The second: If they appear not for the purpose of attestation, the door of proving prophethood by miracle would be closed, because it would be possible that it is for other than the purpose of attestation. And it was refuted by what has passed: that when they are accompanied by a claim, they definitively indicate attestation.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This is the second objection and its refutation. The objectors say: if extraordinary acts can occur without the purpose of confirming a prophet's truth (tasdiq), then we cannot prove prophethood by miracles, because any miracle could be just a karamah. The refutation, as previously established, is that a miracle (mu'jizah) is specifically the one that accompanies a claim of prophethood and challenges opponents. In that context, it gives certain attestation. So the objection fails because karamat occur without such a claim, preserving the distinctive role of prophets' miracles.

Key Terms
غرض التصديق
إثبات النبوة
مقارنة الدعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If saints can perform miracles, then we cannot distinguish prophets.
Correction: The key is the accompanying claim and challenge; miracles are only those acts paired with a claim of prophethood.

Checkpoint
Why does the second objection not refute the possibility of karamat?
A) Because karamat are impossible
B) Because miracles are only given to prophets and are accompanied by a claim
C) Because awliya are more powerful
D) Because the Quran prohibits miracles for saints

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the definition of a miracle includes its accompaniment of a prophetic claim, which karamat lack.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Do Karamat Diminish the Status of Prophets?
Objection that karamat diminish prophets' status, and refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third objection that karamat diminish prophets' status is refuted by demonstrating that karamat actually enhance the prophets' stature and lead people to Islam.

Core Idea
Karamat do not diminish prophets; they magnify them by showing the power of following their way.

This lesson presents the third objection against the reality of karamat—that saints performing extraordinary acts undermines the unique rank of prophets. The refutation clarifies that karamat actually increase respect for prophets and often bring people to Islam. Before examining the source passage, we will consider the underlying concern and the logical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objectors' claim: that sharing extraordinary acts with prophets diminishes the prophets' greatness and standing in hearts.
This is a common concern about the uniqueness of prophethood.
2
Examine the refutation
The refutation denies the premise and argues that karamat increase the majesty of prophets and the desire to follow them.
Notice the phrase 'بل تزيد' (rather it increases).
3
Identify the evidence
The refutation offers two pieces of evidence: followers attain such degrees by following the Sharia, and many disbelievers embraced Islam by witnessing karamat.
This shows the positive impact of karamat.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider how karamat actually highlight the truth of prophethood rather than competing with it.
This refutation turns the objection on its head.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثُ : أنَّ مشاركة الأولياء للأنبياء في ظهورِ الخوارقِ يخلُّ بِعِظَمِ قَدْرِ الأنبياء ، ووقعهم في النفوس . ورُدَّ بالمنع ، بل تزيد في جلالةِ أقدارهم ، والرغبة في اتباعهم؛ حيثُ نالَتْ أَممُهم وأتباعُهم مثل هذهِ الدرجة؛ ببركة الاقتداء بشريعتهم، والاستقامة على طريقتهم، وقد أسلم بمشاهدة أولياء الله تعالى ومشاهدة كراماتِهم خَلْقُ كثيرٌ مِنَ الكفرة
The third: That the sharing of the awliya with the prophets in the appearance of extraordinary events undermines the greatness of the prophets and their standing in hearts. And it was refuted by denial, rather it increases the majesty of their status and the desire to follow them, since their nations and followers attained such a degree by virtue of following their Sharia and upholding their way. And many of the disbelievers have embraced Islam by witnessing the awliya of Allah and witnessing their karamat.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This is the third objection and its refutation. The objectors claim that if awliya also perform extraordinary acts, it reduces the unique status of prophets in people's hearts. The refutation is straightforward: in fact, karamat enhance the prophets' stature because they demonstrate the excellence of the prophets' teachings — their followers, by adhering to the Sharia, reach such high levels that even extraordinary acts appear. Moreover, many people have converted to Islam by seeing the karamas of saints, which leads them to believe in the truth of Islam and the prophets. Thus, karamat actually increase respect and desire to follow the prophets.

Key Terms
مشاركة الأولياء
عظم قدر الأنبياء
بركة الاقتداء
الخوارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that karamat compete with prophethood and diminish the prophets' status.
Correction: In reality, karamat highlight the truth of prophethood by showing the fruits of following the Sharia, and they lead people to Islam.

Checkpoint

A) By denying that awliya perform miracles
B) By asserting that prophets themselves gain fame from karamat
C) By showing that karamat are evidence of the prophets' excellence and lead people to Islam
D) By saying prophets are always known directly

Key Takeaway
Karamat do not reduce the prophets' stature; rather, they magnify it by demonstrating the power of following the prophetic way and by leading people to embrace Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about extraordinary events attributed to saints, do you ever feel a twinge of jealousy or feel that it somehow lessens the uniqueness of the Prophet? What does this lesson reveal about that feeling, and how can you actively honor the Prophet's role as the source of all such blessings?
?

?

Fourth Objection: News of the Unseen and Editorial Note on Count
Editorial note on the fourth objection and count of objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that this passage is an editorial note about the fourth objection regarding news of the unseen, and that the number of objections was condensed from five to four by the author.

Core Idea
Editorial notes can interrupt original arguments and reflect scholarly condensation.

This passage presents a puzzling case. It begins by introducing the fourth objection concerning news of the unseen, but then it abruptly moves into a discussion about the number of objections and a reference to walking from Bukhara to Mecca. It becomes clear that this is not a full objection but rather an editorial comment inserted into the text. Understanding this helps us appreciate the scholarly tradition of revisiting and condensing arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, noting the shift from 'fourth objection' to a discussion about al-Nasafi and the number of objections.
The text uses parentheses and references that indicate a change in topic.
2
Identify the two parts
Distinguish the incomplete objection about news of the unseen from the editorial note that follows. The note explains that the author condensed the objections from five to four.
3
Reflect on the editorial purpose
Consider why the author might have shortened the objections. This shows how later scholars sometimes refine earlier arguments.
This connects to the broader theme of scholarly development.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع ـ وهو حال خاص بالإخبار عن المغيبات - : قوله تعالى : الأولياء في عصره، وقد نقل هذا عنه الإمام ابن العلاء في « فتاواه »، ونقل فيها عن القاضي الإمام صدر الإسلام أبي يسر البزدوي في أصول التوحيد: أن المشي من بخارى إلى مكة في ليلة واحدة من جملة الكرامات )، والإمام النسفي هو نفسه صاحب ( العقائد ( المنسوبة إليه، والتي شرحها العلامة السعد التفتازاني رحمهما الله تعالى (۱) وهذا هو الوجه الأول من وجوه الاعتراض، وقد نعته العلامة السعد بأنه العمدة عندهم، وقد تصرّف الإمام المصنف في اختزال هذه الوجوه فعدَّها أربعة، وهي عند العلامة السعد خمسة
The fourth — and it is a specific case regarding news of the unseen — : His saying, the Exalted: the awliya in his era, and this has been transmitted from him by Imam Ibn al-‘Ala in his Fatawa, and he transmitted therein from the Qadi Imam Sadr al-Islam Abu Yusr al-Bazdawi in Usul al-Tawhid: that walking from Bukhara to Mecca in one night is among the karamat. And Imam al-Nasafi is himself the author of al-‘Aqa'id attributed to him, which was commented upon by al-‘Allamah al-Sa‘d al-Taftazani, may Allah have mercy on them. And this is the first of the objections, and al-‘Allamah al-Sa‘d described it as the main one according to them. And the authoring Imam condensed these objections, counting them as four, while according to al-‘Allamah al-Sa‘d they are five.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is somewhat disjointed. It begins by stating the fourth objection, which concerns the specific case of informing about the unseen (ghaybiyat). However, the text then quotes 'His saying, the Exalted' but does not give a verse; instead it provides a narration about al-Nasafi and other scholars, stating that walking from Bukhara to Mecca in one night is among the karamat. Then it identifies al-Nasafi as the author of al-‘Aqa'id. Finally, it notes that the author (al-Sanusi or the commentator) reduced the five objections to four, whereas al-Taftazani had five, and that the first objection is considered the principal one. This seems more like an editorial note than a continuous objection-refutation pair. The fourth objection itself is not fully presented; the text seems to change topic. It is likely that the original text had a different structure, and this passage reflects a commentary or summary.

Key Terms
إخبار عن المغيبات
المشي من بخارى إلى مكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) There are seven objections
B) The author counted four, but al-Taftazani had five
C) There are only two objections
D) The objections are all invalid

Key Takeaway
This passage highlights that editorial notes can obscure original arguments, and that scholarly works often condense or modify earlier objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Miracle of Bird Flight and the Crossing of the Sirat
Author argues that crossing the Sirat is possible, using the example of bird flight as a visible miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed miracle of bird flight demonstrates the possibility of crossing the Sirat (bridge) in the afterlife, and how the author uses empirical evidence to support theological claims.

Core Idea
The miracle of bird flight proves that crossing the Sirat is within God's power.

The author presents a compelling argument from the natural world to affirm the possibility of a key eschatological event: crossing the Sirat. By examining how birds fly in ways that defy ordinary explanation, he shows that the miraculous is not only possible but observable. This lesson unpacks that argument and connects it to the hadith about crossing the bridge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the author's flow: he starts with bird flight, then applies it to the Sirat.
2
Identify the three types of bird flight
The author describes three ways birds fly. Find them in the passage and note how each type breaks the customary norm in a different way.
These observations serve as empirical evidence.
3
Understand the author's conclusion
After the types, the author concludes that neither the wings nor their movement cause flight—it is Allah directly. Grasp how he moves from observation to theological conclusion.
4
Apply to the Sirat crossing
The author then quotes a hadith about different ways people cross the Sirat. See how he uses the bird example to argue that such a crossing is not impossible.
The supplication at the end reinforces the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والطيران في الهواء ، غايته : مخالفة العادة ، ولا شكّ أنَّ الآخرة أكثر أحوالها الخوارقُ ، كيف وقد شاهدنا في الدنيا حسّاً ما يُؤكِّدُ ذلك ؟! فإنَّ الطير في الهواءِ يُسيِّرُهُ اللهُ تعالى فيهِ على ثلاثةِ أنواع : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّهُ يذهبُ في الهواء قابضاً جناحيهِ يُحرِّكُهما والثاني : أنَّه يذهب فاتحاً جناحيه غيرَ مُحرِّكهما : والثالثُ : ما شاهدناه يذهبُ في سرعة شديدة قابضاً جناحيه لا مُحرَّكَهما ولا ناشرهما • آخر وإذا شُوهد هذا المعنى استبانَ بالمشاهدةِ أَنَّهُ لا أثر للجناح ولا لتحريكه في الطيرانِ ، ولا لجسم ثقيل في استقرار عليهِ ، وإِنَّما الله سبحانَهُ يمسك ما شاءَ ويُسيَّرُ ما شاءَ كيف يشاء فكيفَ يُستبعد مرور الخلائقِ على الصراط على الوصفِ الذي وصفَهُ بهِ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ؟! ثم إنَّ اللهَ تعالى يُسهَلُ الصراطَ على مَنْ أرادَ ، كما جاءَ في الحديث : أنَّ منهم مَنْ يَمُرُّ كالبرق الخاطف ، ومنهم مَنْ يمرُّ كالريح الهايَّةِ ، ومنهم مَنْ يمرُّ كالجوادِ ، ومنهم مَنْ تخرُّ رجلاه وتتعلَّقُ يداهُ (1) ومنهم مَنْ يخرُّ على وجهه 1 اللهمَّ ، يا ذا الفضل العظيم ، والخيرِ الجسيم ؛ ثبت أقدامنا عليهِ يوم تزلُّ الأقدامُ ، واجعل مرورنا عليه كالبرق الخاطفِ ، يا أرحمَ الراحمينَ، يا ذا الجلال والإكرام .
And flying in the air, its purpose is: breaking the norm, and there is no doubt that the Hereafter has more miraculous occurrences in its states, how indeed we have witnessed in this world by sense what confirms that?! For the bird in the air, Allah the Exalted causes it to move in it in three types: The first: that it goes in the air contracting its wings, moving them. And the second: that it goes opening its wings without moving them. And the third: what we have seen it goes with extreme speed, contracting its wings, neither moving them nor spreading them. Finally, and when this meaning is observed, it becomes clear by observation that there is no effect of the wing nor of its movement in flying, nor of a heavy body in resting upon it, but rather Allah, glorified be He, holds what He wills and causes to move what He wills as He wills. So how can the passing of creatures over the Sirat (bridge) be considered far-fetched in the description that the Prophet (peace be upon him) described it?! Then Allah the Exalted makes the Sirat easy for whomever He wills, as came in the hadith: that among them are those who pass like a flash of lightning, and among them those who pass like a strong wind, and among them those who pass like a horse, and among them those whose legs stumble and their hands hang (1), and among them those who fall on his face 1. O Allah, O Possessor of immense grace and great goodness; make our feet firm on it on the day when feet slip, and make our crossing upon it like a flash of lightning, O Most Merciful of the merciful, O Possessor of majesty and honor.
Source pages p. 535

Commentary
This passage is an argument from analogy, using observable miracles in nature to demonstrate the possibility of crossing the Sirat (bridge) in the afterlife. The author begins by stating that the purpose of bird flight is to break the customary norm (mukhalafat al-‘ada), establishing that miracles are not unusual in the Hereafter. He then presents empirical evidence: we see birds flying in three distinct ways—with wings moving, with wings open but still, and with wings closed at great speed. From these observations, he concludes that neither the wings nor their movement, nor any physical property, actually causes flight; rather, it is Allah who directly holds and moves birds as He wills. By extension, the crossing of the Sirat, described by the Prophet as involving various speeds and degrees of difficulty, is not impossible. The author then cites a hadith listing different categories of people crossing the Sirat, from the very fast (like lightning) to those who fall. The passage ends with a supplication asking Allah for a swift and steady crossing.

Key Terms
الطيران
مخالفة العادة
الصراط
الخوارق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that bird flight is a purely mechanical process.
Correction: The author argues that flight is a direct act of God; wings and their movement are not the true cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of the author's discussion of bird flight?
A) To explain the physics of flight
B) To prove that crossing the Sirat is not impossible by analogy with a miracle in nature
C) To show that birds are angels
D) To argue against the existence of wings

Key Takeaway
By observing the miracle of bird flight, we understand that crossing the Sirat is possible through God's direct power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every movement you make, including the beating of your heart, is directly caused by Allah, or do you still attribute it to your own strength and skill? How would your daily actions change if you internalized this lesson?
?
The author argues that bird flight is a direct act of Allah and not due to wings or physical laws. What would it mean for your life if you saw every natural event, including your own breathing, as a direct miracle from Him? How would that affect your gratitude and your reliance on Him?
?
If crossing the Sirat is as easy for Allah as making a bird fly, what is your greatest fear about that day? Is your fear of the difficulty of the crossing, or is it your own sins that make it terrifying? What is one concrete change you can make today to increase your hope of passing like lightning?
